%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
\chapter{Unit 8: The Limit of a Function}
\label{chap:unit8}
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%

%%===============================================================
\section{The Limit of a Function}
\label{sec:the-limit-of-a-function}
%%===============================================================

In preliminary calculus, the concept of a limit is probably the most difficult one to grasp (after all, it took mathematicians 150 years to arrive at it); it is also the most important and most useful one.

The intuitive definition of a limit is inadequate to prove anything rigorously about it. The problem lies in the vague term "arbitrarily close". We discussed earlier that the meaning of this term is that the closer $x$ gets to the specified value, the closer the function must get to the limit, so that however close we want the function to the limit, we can accomplish this by making $x$ sufficiently close to our value. We can express this requirement technically as follows:

% [Image: Whenever a point $x$ is within $\delta$ units of $c$ , $f(x)$ is within $\varepsilon$ units of $L$]

\paragraph{Formal definition of a limit}


Let $f(x)$ be a function defined on an open interval $D$ that contains $c$ , except possibly at $x=c$ . Let $L$ be a number. Then we say that
$$\lim\_{x\to c}f(x)=L$$
if, for every $\varepsilon>0$ , there exists a $\delta>0$ such that for all $x\in D$ with
$$0<|x-c|<\delta$$
we have
$$\Big|f(x)-L\Big|<\varepsilon$$

To further explain, earlier we said that "however close we want the function to the limit, we can find a corresponding $x$ close to our value." Using our new notation of epsilon ($\varepsilon$) and delta ($\delta$), we mean that if we want to make $f(x)$ within $\varepsilon$ of $L$ , the limit, then we know that making $x$ within $\delta$ of $c$ puts it there.

Again, since this is tricky, let's resume our example from before: $f(x)=x^2$ , at $x=2$. To start, let's say we want $f(x)$ to be within 0.01 of the limit. We know by now that the limit should be 4, so we say: for $\varepsilon=0.01$ , there is some $\delta$ so that as long as $0<|x-c|<\delta$ , then $\Big|f(x)-L\Big|<\varepsilon$ .

To show this, we can pick \textit{any} $\delta$ that is bigger than 0, so long as it works. For example, you might pick $10^{-14}$ , because you are absolutely sure that if $x$ is within $10^{-14}$ of 2, then $f(x)$ will be within 0.01 of 4. This $\delta$ works for $\varepsilon=0.01$ . But we can't just pick a specific value for $\varepsilon$ , like 0.01, because we said in our definition "for \textit{every} $\varepsilon>0$" . This means that we need to be able to show an infinite number of $\delta$s, one for each $\varepsilon$ . We can't list an infinite number of $\delta$s!

Of course, we know of a very good way to do this; we simply create a function, so that for every $\varepsilon$ , it can give us a $\delta$ . In this case, one definition of $\delta$ that works is $\delta(\varepsilon)=
\begin{cases}
2\sqrt2-2\&\text{if }\varepsilon\ge4 \\\\ \sqrt{\varepsilon+4}-2\&\text{if }\varepsilon0$ and show that whenever $0<|x-c|<\delta$ we have $\Big|f(x)-L\Big|0$ there exists a $\delta$ such that whenever
$$x>\delta$$
we have
$$\Big|f(x)-L\Big|<\varepsilon$$
When this holds we write
$$\lim\_{x\to\infty}f(x)=L$$
or
$$f(x)\to L \text{ as } x\to\infty$$
Similarly, we call $L$ the \textbf{limit} of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $-\infty$ if for every number $\varepsilon>0$ , there exists a number $\delta$ such that whenever $x<\delta$ we have
$$\Big|f(x)-L\Big|<\varepsilon$$
When this holds we write
$$\lim\_{x\to-\infty}f(x)=L$$
or
$$f(x)\to L \text{ as } x\to-\infty$$

Notice the difference in these two definitions. For the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $\infty$ we are interested in those $x$ such that $x>\delta$ . For the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $-\infty$ we are interested in those $x$ such that $x<\delta$ .

\paragraph{Examples}


Here are some examples of the formal definition.

\textbf{Example 1}

We know from earlier in the chapter that
$$\lim\_{x\to8}\frac{x}{4}=2$$
What is $\delta$ when $\varepsilon=0.01$ for this limit?

We start with the desired conclusion and substitute the given values for $f(x)$ and $\varepsilon$:
$$\left|\frac{x}{4}-2\right|0$ such that:

$$\left|\sin\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)-L\right|<\varepsilon=|1-L|$$

for every $x$ with $0<|x|<\delta$. But, for every $\delta>0$, there exists some (possibly very large) $n$ such that:

$$0a)f(x))=lim(x->a)ln(f(x))? -->

\paragraph{Formal Definition of a Limit Being Infinity}


Let $f(x)$ be a function defined on an open interval $D$ that contains $c$, except possibly at $x=c$. Then we say that:

$$\lim\_{x\to c}f(x)=\infty$$

if, for every $\varepsilon$, there exists a $\delta>0$ such that for all $x\in D$ with:

$$0<|x-c|<\delta$$

we have:

$$f(x)>\varepsilon$$

When this holds we write:

$$\lim\_{x\to c}f(x)=\infty$$

or

$$f(x)\to\infty \text{ as } x\to c$$

Similarly, we say that:

$$\lim\_{x\to c}f(x)=-\infty$$

if, for every $\varepsilon$, there exists a $\delta>0$ such that for all $x\in D$ with:

$$0<|x-c|<\delta$$

we have:

$$f(x)<\varepsilon$$

When this holds we write:

$$\lim\_{x\to c}f(x)=-\infty$$

or

$$f(x)\to-\infty \text{ as } x\to c$$

\paragraph{Exact Integrals as Limits of Sums}


Using the definition of an integral, we can evaluate the limit as $n$ goes to infinity. This technique requires a fairly high degree of familiarity with summation identities. This technique is often referred to as evaluation "by definition," and can be used to find definite integrals, as long as the integrands are fairly simple. We start with the definition of the integral:

$$\int\limits\_a^b f(x)dx = \lim\_{n\to\infty}\left[\frac{b-a}{n}\sum\_{k=1}^n f(x\_k^*)\right]$$

Then picking $x\_k^*$ to be $x\_k=a+k\frac{b-a}{n}$ we get:

$$\int\limits\_a^b f(x)dx = \lim\_{n\to\infty}\left[\frac{b-a}{n}\sum\_{k=1}^n f\big(a+k\tfrac{b-a}{n}\big)\right]$$

In some simple cases, this expression can be reduced to a real number, which can be interpreted as the area under the curve if $f(x)$ is positive on $[a,b]$.

\paragraph{Example 1}


Find
$$
\int\_0^2 x^2 \, dx
$$
by writing the integral as a limit of Riemann sums.

$$
\int\_0^2 x^2 \, dx = \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{b-a}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n f(x\_k^*) \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{2}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n f \left( \frac{2k}{n} \right) \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{2}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n \left( \frac{2k}{n} \right)^2 \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{2}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n \frac{4k^2}{n^2} \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{8}{n^3} \sum\_{k=1}^n k^2 \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{8}{n^3} \cdot \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{4}{3} \cdot \frac{2n^2 + 3n + 1}{n^2} \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{8}{3} + \frac{4}{n} + \frac{4}{3n^2} \right]
$$

$$
= \frac{8}{3}
$$

In other cases, it is even possible to evaluate indefinite integrals using the formal definition. We can define the indefinite integral as follows:

$$
\int f(x) \, dx = \int\_0^x f(t) \, dt = \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x-0}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n f(t\_k^*) \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n f \left( 0 + \frac{k(x-0)}{n} \right) \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n f \left( \frac{kx}{n} \right) \right]
$$

\paragraph{Example 2}


Suppose $f(x) = x^2$, then we can evaluate the indefinite integral as follows.

$$
\int\_0^x f(t) \, dt = \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n f \left( \frac{kx}{n} \right) \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n \left( \frac{kx}{n} \right)^2 \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x}{n} \sum\_{k=1}^n \frac{k^2 \cdot x^2}{n^2} \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x^3}{n^3} \sum\_{k=1}^n k^2 \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x^3}{n^3} \cdot \frac{n(n+1)(2n+1)}{6} \right]
$$

$$
= \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{x^3}{n^3} \cdot \frac{2n^3 + 3n^2 + n}{6} \right]
$$

$$
= x^3 \cdot \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{2n^3}{6n^3} + \frac{3n^2}{6n^3} + \frac{n}{6n^3} \right]
$$

$$
= x^3 \cdot \lim\_{n \to \infty} \left[ \frac{1}{3} + \frac{1}{2n} + \frac{1}{6n^2} \right]
$$

$$
= x^3 \cdot \frac{1}{3}
$$

$$
= \frac{x^3}{3}
$$

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Apply the Epsilon-Delta Definition of Limits:} Students will be able to use the epsilon-delta definition of limits to rigorously prove that a given function $ f(x) $ approaches a limit $ L $ as $ x $ approaches $ c $. This involves finding a suitable $ \delta $ for any given $ \varepsilon $ to demonstrate that $ |f(x) - L| < \varepsilon $ whenever $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta $.
    \item \textbf{Determine Limits at Infinity:} Students will be able to determine the limit of a function $ f(x) $ as $ x $ approaches $ \infty $ or $ -\infty $ using the formal definition, by showing that for any positive $ \varepsilon $, there exists a $ \delta $ such that $ f(x) $ is within $ \varepsilon $ of the limit when $ x $ is greater than $ \delta $ (or less than $ \delta $ for negative infinity).
    \item \textbf{Evaluate Limits Using Squeeze Theorem:} Students will be able to apply the Squeeze Theorem to find the limit of functions that are bounded between two other functions with known limits, particularly for cases where direct application of the epsilon-delta definition is complex or impractical.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Apply the Epsilon-Delta Definition of Limits}
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Let $f(x) = 3x + 2$. What is the correct $\delta$ to use for proving that $\lim_{x \to 1} f(x) = 5$ given $\varepsilon = 0.1$?
    \item A. $\delta = \frac{0.1}{3}$
    \item B. $\delta = \frac{0.1}{5}$
    \item C. $\delta = \frac{0.1}{3 + 2}$
    \item D. $\delta = \frac{0.1}{3}$
    \item \textbf{True/False:} For the function $f(x) = x^2$, to show $\lim_{x \to 2} f(x) = 4$ using the epsilon-delta definition, you need to find a $\delta$ such that for all $\varepsilon > 0$, if $0 < |x - 2| < \delta$, then $|x^2 - 4| < \varepsilon$. (True/False)
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the epsilon-delta definition to prove that $\lim_{x \to 3} (2x + 1) = 7$. Provide a suitable $\delta$ for a given $\varepsilon > 0$. \textbf{Part 2: Determine Limits at Infinity}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Determine the limit of the function $f(x) = \frac{5x^2 + 3}{2x^2 - 7}$ as $x \to \infty$. Show your reasoning.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find the limit of the function $g(x) = \frac{1}{x}$ as $x \to -\infty$ and explain the approach used to determine this limit.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} For the function $h(x) = \frac{3x^3 - 2x + 1}{x^3 + 4}$, what is the limit as $x \to \infty$?
    \item A. 0
    \item B. 1
    \item C. 3
    \item D. -3 \textbf{Part 3: Evaluate Limits Using Squeeze Theorem}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the Squeeze Theorem to find the limit of $\sin(x) \cdot \frac{1}{x}$ as $x \to \infty$. Explain your reasoning.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} If $0 \leq f(x) \leq x^2$ for all $x$ in an interval around 0, and $\lim_{x \to 0} x^2 = 0$, then by the Squeeze Theorem, $\lim_{x \to 0} f(x) = 0$. (True/False)
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} For the function $f(x) = x \sin\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)$, which of the following is a correct application of the Squeeze Theorem to find $\lim_{x \to 0} f(x)$?
    \item A. Use $-x \leq f(x) \leq x$ and apply the limits as $x \to 0$.
    \item B. Use $0 \leq f(x) \leq 1$ and apply the limits as $x \to 0$.
    \item C. Use $-1 \leq f(x) \leq 1$ and apply the limits as $x \to 0$.
    \item D. Use $x \leq f(x) \leq 2x$ and apply the limits as $x \to 0$.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{The Limit Laws}
\label{sec:the-limit-laws}
%%===============================================================

Now that we have the formal definition of a limit, we can set about proving some of the properties we stated earlier in this chapter about limits.

\paragraph{Constant Rule for Limits}

If $a$ and $b$ are constants, then $\lim\_{x \to a} b = b$.

\textbf{Proof of the Constant Rule for Limits}: We need to find a $\delta > 0$ such that for every $\varepsilon > 0$, $|b - b| < \varepsilon$ whenever $0 < |x - a| < \delta$. Since $|b - b| = 0$ and $\varepsilon > 0$, $|b - b| < \varepsilon$ is satisfied independently of any value of $\delta$; that is, we can choose any $\delta$ we like and the $\varepsilon$ condition holds.

\paragraph{Identity Rule for Limits}

If $a$ is a constant, then $\lim\_{x \to a} x = a$.

\textbf{Proof}: To prove that $\lim\_{x \to a} x = a$, we need to find a $\delta > 0$ such that for every $\varepsilon > 0$, $|x - a| < \varepsilon$ whenever $0 < |x - a| < \delta$. Choosing $\delta = \varepsilon$ satisfies this condition.

\paragraph{Scalar Product Rule for Limits}

Suppose that $\lim\_{x \to a} f(x) = L$ for finite $L$ and that $c$ is constant. Then $\lim\_{x \to a} c \cdot f(x) = c \cdot \lim\_{x \to a} f(x) = c \cdot L$.

\textbf{Proof}: Given the limit above, there exists in particular a $\delta > 0$ such that $\Big|f(x) - L\Big| < \frac{\varepsilon}{k}$ whenever $0 < |x - a| < \delta$, for some $k > 0$ such that $|c| < k$. Hence:

$$
\Big|c \cdot f(x) - c \cdot L\Big| = |c| \cdot \Big|f(x) - L\Big| < k \cdot \frac{\varepsilon}{k} = \varepsilon
$$

\paragraph{Sum Rule for Limits}

Suppose that $\lim\_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = M$. Then,

$$
\lim\_{x \to c} \Big[f(x) + g(x)\Big] = \lim\_{x \to c} f(x) + \lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = L + M
$$

\textbf{Proof}: Since we are given that $\lim\_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = M$, there must be functions, call them $\delta\_f(\varepsilon)$ and $\delta\_g(\varepsilon)$, such that for all $\varepsilon > 0$, $\Big|f(x) - L\Big| < \varepsilon$ whenever $|x - c| < \delta\_f(\varepsilon)$, and $\Big|g(x) - M\Big| < \varepsilon$ whenever $|x - c| < \delta\_g(\varepsilon)$. 

Adding the two inequalities gives:

$$
\Big|f(x) - L\Big| + \big|g(x) - M\big| < 2 \varepsilon
$$

By the triangle inequality, we have:

$$
\bigg|(f(x) - L) + (g(x) - M)\bigg| = \bigg|(f(x) + g(x)) - (L + M)\bigg| \le \Big|f(x) - L\Big| + \Big|g(x) - M\Big|
$$

So we have:

$$
\bigg|(f(x) + g(x)) - (L + M)\bigg| < 2 \varepsilon
$$

whenever $|x - c| < \delta\_f(\varepsilon)$ and $|x - c| < \delta\_g(\varepsilon)$. Let $\delta\_{fg}(\varepsilon)$ be the smaller of $\delta\_f(\frac{\varepsilon}{2})$ and $\delta\_g(\frac{\varepsilon}{2})$. Then this $\delta$ satisfies the definition of a limit for $\lim\_{x \to c} \Big[f(x) + g(x)\Big]$ having limit $L + M$.

\paragraph{Difference Rule for Limits}

Suppose that $\lim\_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = M$. Then,

$$
\lim\_{x \to c} \Big[f(x) - g(x)\Big] = \lim\_{x \to c} f(x) - \lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = L - M
$$

\textbf{Proof}: Define $h(x) = -g(x)$. By the Scalar Product Rule for Limits, $\lim\_{x \to c} h(x) = -M$. Then by the Sum Rule for Limits:

$$
\lim\_{x \to c} \Big[f(x) - g(x)\Big] = \lim\_{x \to c} \Big[f(x) + h(x)\Big] = L - M
$$

\paragraph{Product Rule for Limits}

Suppose that $\lim\_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = M$. Then,

$$
\lim\_{x \to c} \Big[f(x) \cdot g(x)\Big] = \lim\_{x \to c} f(x) \cdot \lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = L \cdot M
$$

\textbf{Proof}: Let $\varepsilon$ be any positive number. The assumptions imply the existence of the positive numbers $\delta\_1$, $\delta\_2$, $\delta\_3$ such that:

1. $\Big|f(x) - L\Big| < \frac{\varepsilon}{2(1 + |M|)}$ when $0 < |x - c| < \delta\_1$
2. $\Big|g(x) - M\Big| < \frac{\varepsilon}{2(1 + |L|)}$ when $0 < |x - c| < \delta\_2$
3. $\Big|g(x) - M\Big| < 1$ when $0 < |x - c| < \delta\_3$

According to condition (3), we see that:

$$
\Big|g(x)\Big| = \bigg|g(x) - M + M\bigg| \le \Big|g(x) - M\Big| + |M| < 1 + |M|
$$

when $0 < |x - c| < \delta\_3$. Supposing then that $0 < |x - c| < \min\{\delta\_1, \delta\_2, \delta\_3\}$ and using (1) and (2), we obtain:

$$
\begin{aligned}
\bigg|f(x) g(x) - L M\bigg| &= \bigg|f(x) g(x) - L g(x) + L g(x) - L M\bigg| \\\\&\le \bigg|f(x) g(x) - L g(x)\bigg| + \bigg|L g(x) - L M\bigg| \\\\&= \Big|g(x)\Big| \cdot \Big|f(x) - L\Big| + |L| \cdot \Big|g(x) - M\Big| \\\\&< (1 + |M|) \frac{\varepsilon}{2(1 + |M|)} + (1 + |L|) \frac{\varepsilon}{2(1 + |L|)} \\\\&= \varepsilon
\end{aligned}
$$

\paragraph{Quotient Rule for Limits}

Suppose that $ \lim\_{x \to c} f(x) = L $ and $ \lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = M $ and $ M \ne 0 $. Then
$$ \lim\_{x \to c} \frac{f(x)}{g(x)} = \frac{\lim\_{x \to c} f(x)}{\lim\_{x \to c} g(x)} = \frac{L}{M} $$

\textbf{Proof:} If we can show that $ \lim\_{x \to c} \frac{1}{g(x)} = \frac{1}{M} $, then we can define a function, $ h(x) $ as $ h(x) = \frac{1}{g(x)} $ and appeal to the Product Rule for Limits to prove the theorem. So we just need to prove that $ \lim\_{x \to c} \frac{1}{g(x)} = \frac{1}{M} $.

Let $ \varepsilon $ be any positive number. The assumptions imply the existence of the positive numbers $ \delta\_1, \delta\_2 $ such that

1. $ \Big| g(x) - M \Big| < \frac{\varepsilon |M|^2}{2} $ when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta\_1 $
2. $ \Big| g(x) - M \Big| < \frac{|M|}{2} $ when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta\_2 $

According to condition (2) we see that
$ |M| = |M - g(x) + g(x)| \le |M - g(x)| + |g(x)| < \frac{|M|}{2} + |g(x)| $
so $ |g(x)| > \frac{|M|}{2} $ when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta\_2 $

which implies that
$ \left| \frac{1}{g(x)} \right| < \frac{2}{|M|} $ when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta\_2 $

Supposing then that $ 0 < |x - c| < \min\{\delta\_1, \delta\_2\} $ and using (1) and (3) we obtain
$$ \begin{aligned}
\left| \frac{1}{g(x)} - \frac{1}{M} \right| &= \left| \frac{M - g(x)}{M g(x)} \right| \\\\&= \left| \frac{g(x) - M}{M g(x)} \right| \\\\&= \left| \frac{1}{g(x)} \right| \cdot \left| \frac{1}{M} \right| \cdot |g(x) - M| \\\\&< \frac{2}{|M|} \cdot \frac{1}{|M|} \cdot |g(x) - M| \\\\&< \frac{2}{|M|} \cdot \frac{1}{|M|} \cdot \frac{\varepsilon |M|^2}{2} \\\\&= \varepsilon
\end{aligned} $$

\paragraph{Squeeze Theorem}

Suppose that $ g(x) \le f(x) \le h(x) $ holds for all $ x $ in some open interval containing $ c $, except possibly at $ x = c $ itself. Suppose also that $ \lim\_{x \to c} g(x) = \lim\_{x \to c} h(x) = L $. Then $ \lim\_{x \to c} f(x) = L $ also.

\textbf{Proof:} From the assumptions, we know that there exists a $ \delta $ such that
$ \Big| g(x) - L \Big| < \varepsilon $
and
$ \Big| h(x) - L \Big| < \varepsilon $
when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta $.

These inequalities are equivalent to
$ L - \varepsilon < g(x) < L + \varepsilon $
and
$ L - \varepsilon < h(x) < L + \varepsilon $
when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta $.

Using what we know about the relative ordering of $ f(x), g(x) $, and $ h(x) $, we have
$ L - \varepsilon < g(x) \le f(x) \le h(x) < L + \varepsilon $
when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta $.

Then
$ -\varepsilon < f(x) - L < \varepsilon $
when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta $.

So
$ \Big| f(x) - L \Big| < \varepsilon $
when $ 0 < |x - c| < \delta $.

\paragraph{Resources}

\begin{itemize}
    \item \href{https://mathresearch.utsa.edu/wikiFiles/MAT1214/The\%20Limit\%20Laws/MAT1214-2.3TheLimitLawsPwPt.pptx}{The Limit Laws} PowerPoint file created by Dr. Sara Shirinkam, UTSA.
    \item \href{https://mathresearch.utsa.edu/wikiFiles/MAT1214/The\%20Limit\%20Laws/MAT1214\_2.3TheLimitLawsWS1.pdf}{The Limit Laws Worksheet}
    \item \href{https://youtu.be/P7G7F3NzPw0}{Properties of Limits} by The Organic Chemistry Tutor
    \item \href{https://youtu.be/v\_Nz6UUQ4HQ}{Limit Laws to Evaluate a Limit, Example 1} by patrickJMT
    \item \href{https://youtu.be/3d7qLvgZp0s}{Limit Laws to Evaluate a Limit, Example 2} by patrickJMT
    \item \href{https://youtu.be/KeAgfb3NZLc}{Limit Laws to Evaluate a Limit, Example 3} by patrickJMT

\paragraph{Licensing}

Content obtained and/or adapted from:
\begin{itemize}
    \item \href{https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Calculus/Proofs\_of\_Some\_Basic\_Limit\_Rules}{Proofs of Some Basic Limit Rules, Wikibooks: Calculus} under a CC BY-SA license
\end{itemize}

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Understanding and Applying the Constant Rule for Limits:} Students will be able to prove and apply the Constant Rule for Limits, which states that the limit of a constant function is the constant itself. Specifically, students will demonstrate that for any constant $b$ and any point $a$, $\lim_{x \to a} b = b$, and they will be able to use this rule in solving limit problems involving constant functions.
    \item \textbf{Proving the Sum and Difference Rules for Limits:} Students will be able to prove and apply the Sum and Difference Rules for Limits. This includes showing that if $\lim_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim_{x \to c} g(x) = M$, then $\lim_{x \to c} [f(x) + g(x)] = L + M$ and $\lim_{x \to c} [f(x) - g(x)] = L - M$. Students will use these rules to evaluate limits involving the sum and difference of functions.
    \item \textbf{Utilizing the Product and Quotient Rules for Limits:} Students will be able to prove and apply the Product and Quotient Rules for Limits. Specifically, students will demonstrate that if $\lim_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim_{x \to c} g(x) = M$, then $\lim_{x \to c} [f(x) \cdot g(x)] = L \cdot M$ and $\lim_{x \to c} \frac{f(x)}{g(x)} = \frac{L}{M}$ (provided $ M \ne 0$). Students will use these rules to solve limit problems involving the product and quotient of functions.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Understanding and Applying the Constant Rule for Limits}
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} If $b$ is a constant and $a$ is any real number, what is the limit of the constant function $b$ as $x$ approaches $a$?
    \item A. $\lim_{x \to a} b = 0$
    \item B. $\lim_{x \to a} b = a$
    \item C. $\lim_{x \to a} b = b$
    \item D. $\lim_{x \to a} b = \infty$
    \item \textbf{True/False:} The Constant Rule for Limits states that $\lim_{x \to a} (5) = 5$ for any real number $a$. (True/False) Explain why or why not.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Prove the Constant Rule for Limits by showing that for any constant function $f(x) = 7$, the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches any point $a$ is equal to 7. \textbf{Part 2: Proving the Sum and Difference Rules for Limits}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Prove that if $\lim_{x \to c} f(x) = L$ and $\lim_{x \to c} g(x) = M$, then $\lim_{x \to c} [f(x) + g(x)] = L + M$. Use specific functions and limits in your proof.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Given that $\lim_{x \to 2} (3x^2 - 4) = 8$ and $\lim_{x \to 2} (x - 1) = 1$, what is $\lim_{x \to 2} [(3x^2 - 4) - (x - 1)]$?
    \item A. $9$
    \item B. $7$
    \item C. $8$
    \item D. $6$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Show that if $\lim_{x \to -1} (x^3 + 2x) = -1$ and $\lim_{x \to -1} (x - 4) = -5$, then $\lim_{x \to -1} [(x^3 + 2x) - (x - 4)]$ can be computed, and provide the result. \textbf{Part 3: Utilizing the Product and Quotient Rules for Limits}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Prove that if $\lim_{x \to 3} f(x) = 2$ and $\lim_{x \to 3} g(x) = 4$, then $\lim_{x \to 3} [f(x) \cdot g(x)] = 8$. Use specific functions to demonstrate the proof.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Given that $\lim_{x \to 5} (x^2 - 1) = 24$ and $\lim_{x \to 5} (x + 2) = 7$, what is $\lim_{x \to 5} \frac{x^2 - 1}{x + 2}$?
    \item A. $3$
    \item B. $4$
    \item C. $6$
    \item D. $7$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Demonstrate how to apply the Quotient Rule for Limits with the functions $f(x) = x^2 + 1$ and $g(x) = x - 2$, given $\lim_{x \to 2} f(x) = 5$ and $\lim_{x \to 2} g(x) = 0$. Show that $\lim_{x \to 2} \frac{f(x)}{g(x)}$ exists, or explain why it does not.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Continuity}
\label{sec:continuity}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{Defining Continuity}


We are now ready to define the concept of a function being
\textbf{continuous}. The idea is that we want to say that a function is
continuous if you can draw its graph without taking your pencil off the
page. But sometimes this will be true for some parts of a graph but not
for others. Therefore, we want to start by defining what it means for a
function to be continuous at \textit{one point}. The definition is simple, now
that we have the concept of limits:

\paragraph{Definition: Continuity at a Point}


If $f(x)$ is defined on an open interval containing $c$ , then
    $f(x)$ is said to be \textbf{continuous at $c$} if and only if

$\lim_{x\to c}f(x)=f(c)$.

Note that for $f$ to be continuous at $c$, the definition in effect
requires three conditions:

1.  that $f$ is defined at $c$ , so $f(c)$ exists,
2.  the limit as $x$ approaches $c$ exists, and
3.  the limit and $f(c)$ are equal.

If any of these do not hold then $f$ is not continuous at $c$ .

The idea of the definition is that the point of the graph corresponding
to $c$ will be close to the points of the graph corresponding to nearby
$x$-values. Now we can define what it means for a function to be
continuous in general, not just at one point.

\paragraph{Definition: Continuity on an Open Interval}


A function is said to be \textbf{continuous on} $(a,b)$ if it is
    continuous at every point of the interval $(a,b)$.

We often use the phrase "the function is continuous" to mean that the
function is continuous at every real number. This would be the same as
saying the function was continuous on $(-\infty,\infty)$, but it is a
bit more convenient to simply say "continuous".

Note that, by what we already know, the limit of a rational,
exponential, trigonometric or logarithmic function at a point is just
its value at that point, so long as it's defined there. So, all such
functions are continuous wherever they're defined. (Of course, they
can't be continuous where they're \textit{not} defined!)

\paragraph{Discontinuities}


A \textbf{discontinuity} is a point where a function is not continuous. There
are lots of possible ways this could happen, of course. Here we'll just
discuss two simple ways.

\paragraph{Removable discontinuities}


The function $f(x)=\frac{x^2-9}{x-3}$ is not continuous at $x=3$ . It is
discontinuous at that point because the fraction then becomes $\frac00$
, which is undefined. Therefore the function fails the first of our
three conditions for continuity at the point 3; 3 is just not in its
domain.

However, we say that this discontinuity is \textbf{removable}. This is
because, if we modify the function at that point, we can eliminate the
discontinuity and make the function continuous. To see how to make the
function $f(x)$ continuous, we have to simplify $f(x)$ , getting
$f(x)=\frac{x^2-9}{x-3}=\frac{(x+3)(x-3)}{(x-3)}=\frac{x+3}{1}\cdot\frac{x-3}{x-3}$
. We can define a new function $g(x)$ where $g(x)=x+3$ . Note that the
function $g(x)$ is not the same as the original function $f(x)$ ,
because $g(x)$ is defined at $x=3$ , while $f(x)$ is not. Thus, $g(x)$
is continuous at $x=3$ , since $\lim_{x\to3}(x+3)=6=g(3)$ . However,
whenever $x\ne3$ , $f(x)=g(x)$ ; all we did to $f$ to get $g$ was to
make it defined at $x=3$ .

In fact, this kind of simplification is often possible with a
discontinuity in a rational function. We can divide the numerator and
the denominator by a common factor (in our example $x-3$) to get a
function which is the same except where that common factor was 0 (in our
example at $x=3$). This new function will be identical to the old except
for being defined at new points where previously we had division by 0.

However, this is not possible in every case. For example, the function
$f(x)=\frac{x-3}{x^2-6x+9}$ has a common factor of $x-3$ in both the
numerator and denominator, but when you simplify you are left with
$g(x)=\frac{1}{x-3}$ , which is still not defined at $x=3$ . In this
case the domain of $f(x)$ and $g(x)$ are the same, and they are equal
everywhere they are defined, so they are in fact the same function. The
reason that $g(x)$ differed from $f(x)$ in the first example was because
we could take it to have a larger domain and not simply that the
formulas defining $f(x)$ and $g(x)$ were different.

\paragraph{Jump discontinuities}


% [Image: Illustration of a jump
discontinuity]

Not all discontinuities can be removed from a function. Consider this
function:

$$k(x)=
\begin{cases}
1&\text{if }x>0 \\\\-1&\text{if }x\le0
\end{cases}$$

Since $\lim_{x\to0}k(x)$ does not exist, there is no way to redefine $k$
at one point so that it will be continuous at 0. These sorts of
discontinuities are called \textit{nonremovable} discontinuities.

Note, however, that both one-sided limits exist; $\lim\_{x\to0^-}k(x)=-1$
and $\lim\_{x\to0^+}k(x)=1$ . The problem is that they are not equal, so
the graph "jumps" from one side of 0 to the other. In such a case, we
say the function has a \textit{jump} discontinuity. (Note that a jump
discontinuity is a kind of nonremovable discontinuity.)

\paragraph{One-Sided Continuity}


Just as a function can have a one-sided limit, a function can be
continuous from a particular side. For a function to be continuous at a
point from a given side, we need the following three conditions:

1.  the function is defined at the point.
2.  the function has a limit from that side at that point.
3.  the one-sided limit equals the value of the function at the point.

A function will be continuous at a point if and only if it is continuous
from both sides at that point. Now we can define what it means for a
function to be continuous on a closed interval.

\paragraph{Continuity on a Closed Interval}


A function is said to be \textbf{continuous on} $[a,b]$ if and only if

1.  it is continuous on $(a,b)$ .
2.  it is continuous from the right at $a$ .
3.  it is continuous from the left at $b$ .

Notice that, if a function is continuous, then it is continuous on every
closed interval contained in its domain.

\paragraph{Intermediate Value Theorem}

A useful theorem regarding continuous functions is the following:

If a function $f$ is continuous on a closed interval $[a,b]$ , then
    for every value $y$ between $f(a)$ and $f(b)$ there is a value
    $c\in(a,b)$ such that $f(c)=y$.

\paragraph{Application: bisection method}


% [Image: A few steps of the bisection method applied over the starting range $[a\_1,b\_1]$. The bigger red dot is the root of the function.]

The bisection method is the simplest and most reliable algorithm to find
zeros of a continuous function.

Suppose we want to solve the equation $f(x)=0$ . Given two points $a$
and $b$ such that $f(a)$ and $f(b)$ have opposite signs, the
intermediate value theorem tells us that $f$ must have at least one root
between $a$ and $b$ as long as $f$ is continuous on the interval
$[a,b]$. If we know $f$ is continuous in general (say, because it's
made out of rational, trigonometric, exponential and logarithmic
functions), then this will work so long as $f$ is defined at all points
between $a$ and $b$ . So, let's divide the interval $[a,b]$ in two by
computing $c=\frac{a+b}{2}$ . There are now three possibilities:

1.  $f(c)=0$ ,
2.  $f(a)$ and $f(c)$ have opposite signs, or
3.  $f(c)$ and $f(b)$ have opposite signs.

In the first case, we're done. In the second and third cases, we can
repeat the process on the sub-interval where the sign change occurs. In
this way we hone in to a small sub-interval containing the 0. The
midpoint of that small sub-interval is usually taken as a good
approximation to the 0.

Note that, unlike the methods you may have learned in algebra, this
works for \textit{any} continuous function that you (or your calculator) know
how to compute.

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Define and Apply Continuity at a Point:} Students will be able to define what it means for a function to be continuous at a specific point and demonstrate this by evaluating functions to verify continuity using the definition: $\lim_{x \to c} f(x) = f(c)$.
    \item \textbf{Distinguish Between Removable and Nonremovable Discontinuities:} Students will be able to identify and differentiate between removable and nonremovable discontinuities in functions. They will also apply methods to simplify functions to address removable discontinuities and explain why some discontinuities cannot be removed.
    \item \textbf{Use the Intermediate Value Theorem and the Bisection Method:} Students will be able to state and apply the Intermediate Value Theorem to prove the existence of roots within a closed interval for continuous functions. They will also utilize the bisection method to approximate zeros of continuous functions and describe the process and its effectiveness.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Define and Apply Continuity at a Point}
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following statements correctly describes the definition of continuity of a function $f(x)$ at a point $c$?
    \item A. $f(x)$ is continuous at $c$ if $\lim_{x \to c} f(x) = \infty$.
    \item B. $f(x)$ is continuous at $c$ if $\lim_{x \to c} f(x) = f(c)$.
    \item C. $f(x)$ is continuous at $c$ if $f(c)$ exists but $\lim_{x \to c} f(x)$ does not exist.
    \item D. $f(x)$ is continuous at $c$ if $f(c)$ is undefined.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} A function $f(x)$ is continuous at $x = 2$ if and only if $\lim_{x \to 2} f(x) = f(2)$. (True/False) Provide a brief explanation.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Determine whether the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$ is continuous at $x = 2$. If not, use the definition of continuity to explain why and how the function can be simplified to check continuity at this point. \textbf{Part 2: Distinguish Between Removable and Nonremovable Discontinuities}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Identify whether the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1}$ has a removable or nonremovable discontinuity at $x = 1$. Simplify the function to determine the nature of the discontinuity.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following functions has a nonremovable discontinuity at $x = 0$?
    \item A. $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$
    \item B. $f(x) = \frac{x^2 + 2x + 1}{x + 1}$
    \item C. $f(x) = \frac{1}{x}$
    \item D. $f(x) = \frac{x^3 - x}{x^2 - 1}$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} For the function $g(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x^2 - 2x}$, identify and classify the discontinuities. Determine which ones are removable and explain why some discontinuities cannot be removed. \textbf{Part 3: Use the Intermediate Value Theorem and the Bisection Method}
    \item \textbf{True/False:} According to the Intermediate Value Theorem, if a function $f(x)$ is continuous on the interval $[a, b]$ and $N$ is a number between $f(a)$ and $f(b)$, then there exists a $c$ in $[a, b]$ such that $f(c) = N$. (True/False) Justify your answer.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Given the function $f(x) = x^3 - 4x - 2$ and the interval $[1, 2]$, use the Intermediate Value Theorem to determine if there is a root in this interval. Which of the following statements is correct?
    \item A. There is a root in $[1, 2]$ because $f(1) < 0$ and $f(2) > 0$.
    \item B. There is no root in $[1, 2]$ because $f(1) > 0$ and $f(2) < 0$.
    \item C. There is a root in $[1, 2]$ because $f(1) = 0$.
    \item D. The Intermediate Value Theorem does not apply to this function.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the bisection method to approximate a root of the function $h(x) = x^2 - 3$ in the interval $[1, 2]$. Perform two iterations of the bisection method and describe the results.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Limits at Infinity and Asymptotes}
\label{sec:limits-at-infinity-and-asymptotes}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{Infinite Limits}


Another kind of limit involves looking at what happens to $f(x)$ as $x$
gets very big. For example, consider the function $f(x)=\frac{1}{x}$ .
As $x$ gets very big, $\frac{1}{x}$ gets very small. In fact,
$\frac{1}{x}$ gets closer and closer to 0 the bigger $x$ gets. Without
limits it is very difficult to talk about this fact, because $x$ can
keep getting bigger and bigger and $\frac{1}{x}$ never actually gets to
0; but the language of limits exists precisely to let us talk about the
behavior of a function as it approaches something - without caring about
the fact that it will never get there. In this case, however, we have
the same problem as before: how big does $x$ have to be to be sure that
$f(x)$ is really going towards 0?

In this case, we want to say that, however close we want $f(x)$ to get
to 0, for $x$ big enough $f(x)$ is guaranteed to get that close. So we
have yet another definition.

\paragraph{Definition of a limit at infinity}


We call $L$ the \textbf{limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches infinity} if
    $f(x)$ becomes \textbf{arbitrarily close} to $L$ \textbf{whenever} $x$ is
    \textbf{sufficiently large}.

When this holds we write

$$\lim_{x\to\infty}f(x)=L$$ or

$$f(x)\to L\quad as \quad x\to\infty$$
 Similarly, we call $L$ the
\textbf{limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches negative infinity} if $f(x)$
becomes \textbf{arbitrarily close} to $L$ \textbf{whenever} $x$ is **sufficiently
negative**.

When this holds we write

$$\lim_{x\to-\infty}f(x)=L$$ or

$$f(x)\to L\quad as \quad x\to-\infty$$

So, in this case, we write:

$$\quad\lim_{x\to\infty}\frac{1}{x}=0$$ and say "The limit, as $x$
approaches infinity, equals $0$ ," or "as $x$ approaches infinity, the
function approaches 0.

We can also write:

$$\lim_{x\to-\infty}\frac{1}{x}=0$$ because making $x$ very negative
also forces $\frac{1}{x}$ to be close to $0$ .

\textbf{Notice}, however, that infinity is not a number; it's just shorthand
for saying "no matter how big." Thus, this is not the same as the
regular limits we learned about in the last two chapters.

\paragraph{Limits at Infinity of Rational Function}


One special case that comes up frequently is when we want to find the
limit at $\infty$ (or $-\infty$) of a rational function. A rational
function is just one made by dividing two polynomials by each other. For
example, $f(x)=\frac{x^3+x-6}{x^2-4x+3}$ is a rational function. Also,
any polynomial is a rational function, since $1$ is just a (very simple)
polynomial, so we can write the function $f(x)=x^2-3$ as
$f(x)=\frac{x^2-3}{1}$ , the quotient of two polynomials.

Consider the numerator of a rational function as we allow the variable
to grow very large (in either the positive or negative sense). The term
with the highest exponent on the variable will dominate the numerator,
and the other terms become more and more insignificant compared to the
dominating term. The same applies to the denominator. In the limit, the
other terms become negligible, and we only need to examine the
dominating term in the numerator and denominator.

There is a simple rule for determining a limit of a rational function as
the variable approaches infinity. Look for the term with the highest
exponent on the variable in the numerator. Look for the same in the
denominator. This rule is based on that information.

\begin{itemize}
    \item If the exponent of the highest term in the numerator matches the
\end{itemize}
    exponent of the highest term in the denominator, the limit (at both
    $\infty$ and $-\infty$) is the ratio of the coefficients of the
    highest terms.

<!-- -->

\begin{itemize}
    \item If the \textit{numerator} has the highest term, then the fraction is called
\end{itemize}
    "top-heavy". If, when you divide the \textit{numerator} by the
    \textit{denominator} the resulting exponent on the variable is even, then
    the limit (at both $\infty$ and $-\infty$) is $\infty$ . If it is
    odd, then the limit at $\infty$ is $\infty$ , and the limit at
    $-\infty$ is $-\infty$ .

<!-- -->

\begin{itemize}
    \item If the \textit{denominator} has the highest term, then the fraction is
\end{itemize}
    called "bottom-heavy" and the limit at both $\pm\infty$ is 0.

Note that, if the numerator or denominator is a constant (including 1,
as above), then this is the same as $x^0$ . Also, a straight power of
$x$ , like $x^3$ , has coefficient 1, since it is the same as $1x^3$ .

\paragraph{Examples}


Example 1

Find $\lim_{x\to\infty}\frac{x-5}{x-3}$ .

The function $f(x)=\frac{x-5}{x-3}$ is the quotient of two polynomials,
$x-5$ and $x-3$ . By our rule we look for the term with highest exponent
in the numerator; it's $x$ . The term with highest exponent in the
denominator is also $x$ . So, the limit is the ratio of their
coefficients. Since $x=1x$, both coefficients are 1,
$\lim_{x\to\infty}\frac{x-5}{x-3}=\frac11=1$ .

Example 2

Find $\lim_{x\to\infty}\frac{x^3+x-6}{x^2-4x+3}$.

Factoring out $x^3$ (the term with the highest power), we get
$\frac{x^3(1+\frac{1}{x^2}-\frac{6}{x^3})}{x^3(\frac{1}{x}-\frac{4}{x^2}+\frac{3}{x^3})} = \frac{1+\frac{1}{x^2}-\frac{6}{x^3}}{\frac{1}{x}-\frac{4}{x^2}+\frac{3}{x^3}}$

We look at the terms with the highest exponents; for the numerator, it
is $x^3$, while for the denominator it is $x^2$. Since the exponent on
the numerator is higher, we know the limit at $\infty$ will be $\infty$.
We can see by factoring out and canceling the highest power that the
denominator will go to 0, while the top will be a positive 1. So,

$$\lim_{x\to\infty}\frac{x^3+x-6}{x^2-4x+3}=+\infty$$.

\paragraph{Asymptotes}


% [Image: $f(x) = \frac{1}{x-1}$ has a vertical asymptote at $x = 1$ and a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$]

When a function has a positive, finite limit L as $x\to\infty$ or
$x\to-\infty$, we say that the function has a horizontal asymptote at
$y = L$. A horizontal asymptote is a horizontal line on a graph that a
function approaches as $x$ goes to $\pm\infty$. For example, consider
the function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x-1}$. $\lim_{x\to\infty}\frac{1}{x}=0$,
so there is a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$.

When a function approaches infinity or negative infinity as $x\to c$ for
some finite value $c$, we say that the function has a vertical asymptote
at $x = c$. A vertical asymptote is a vertical on a graph that a
function approaches, but never reaches. For example, consider the
function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x-1}$. $\lim\_{x\to 1^{+}}\frac{1}{x}=\infty$
and $\lim\_{x\to 1^{-}}\frac{1}{x}=-\infty$, so there is a vertical
asymptote at $x = 1$ (where the denominator goes to 0).

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Calculate Limits at Infinity:} Students will be able to find the limit of a function as $x$ approaches $\pm\infty$ for both polynomial and rational functions, and interpret the result.
    \item \textbf{Determine Horizontal Asymptotes:} Students will be able to identify and describe the horizontal asymptote of a function based on its limit as $x$ approaches $\pm\infty$.
    \item \textbf{Analyze Vertical Asymptotes:} Students will be able to determine the presence and location of vertical asymptotes by analyzing the behavior of a function as $ x$ approaches a finite value where the function goes to $\pm\infty$.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Calculate Limits at Infinity}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Find the limit of the function $f(x) = \frac{3x^2 - 5x + 2}{2x^2 + 7}$ as $x$ approaches $\infty$. Show your work and provide the result.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What is the limit of the function $g(x) = \frac{5}{x - 1}$ as $x$ approaches $-\infty$?
    \item A. $0$
    \item B. $5$
    \item C. $-\infty$
    \item D. $\infty$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Determine the limit of the function $h(x) = \frac{x^3 - 4x}{2x^3 + 3}$ as $x$ approaches $-\infty$. Explain your reasoning. \textbf{Part 2: Determine Horizontal Asymptotes}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} For the function $f(x) = \frac{4x^2 + 3x}{2x^2 - x + 1}$, determine the horizontal asymptote, if any. Justify your answer based on the limit as $x$ approaches $\infty$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following functions has a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$?
    \item A. $f(x) = \frac{3x + 1}{2x - 4}$
    \item B. $g(x) = \frac{x^2}{x^2 + 1}$
    \item C. $h(x) = \frac{1}{x}$
    \item D. $k(x) = \frac{x^2 + 1}{x}$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find and describe the horizontal asymptote of the function $f(x) = \frac{2x - 5}{x^2 + 3}$. Explain how you determined the asymptote. \textbf{Part 3: Analyze Vertical Asymptotes}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Determine the location and presence of vertical asymptotes for the function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x^2 - 1}$. Show your work to find where the function approaches $\pm\infty$.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} A function $f(x)$ has a vertical asymptote at $x = a$ if $\lim_{x \to a^+} f(x) = \infty$ or $\lim_{x \to a^-} f(x) = -\infty$. (True/False) Provide a brief explanation.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Analyze the function $g(x) = \frac{3x + 2}{x^2 - 4}$ and identify the vertical asymptotes, if any. Describe how you found their locations.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Defining the Derivative}
\label{sec:defining-the-derivative}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{The Definition of Slope}


Historically, the primary motivation for the study of
\textbf{differentiation} was the tangent line problem: for a given curve,
find the slope of the straight line that is tangent to the curve at a
given point. The word \textit{tangent} comes from the Latin word \textit{tangens},
which means touching. Thus, to solve the tangent line problem, we need
to find the slope of a line that is "touching" a given curve at a
given point, or, in modern language, that has the same slope. But what
exactly do we mean by "slope" for a curve?

The solution is obvious in some cases: for example, a line $y=mx+c$ is
its own tangent; the slope at any point is $m$ . For the parabola
$y=x^2$ , the slope at the point $(0,0)$ is $0$ ; the tangent line is
horizontal.

But how can you find the slope of, say, $y=\sin(x)+x^2$ at $x=1.5$? This
is in general a nontrivial question, but first we will deal carefully
with the slope of lines.

\paragraph{The Slope of a Line}


% [Image: Three lines with different
slopes]

The \textbf{slope} of a line, also called the \textbf{gradient} of the line, is a
measure of its inclination. A line that is horizontal has slope 0, a
line from the bottom left to the top right has a positive slope and a
line from the top left to the bottom right has a negative slope.

The slope can be defined in two (equivalent) ways. The first way is to
express it as how much the line climbs for a given motion horizontally.
We denote a change in a quantity using the symbol $\Delta$ (pronounced
"delta"). Thus, a change in $x$ is written as $\Delta x$ . We can
therefore write this definition of slope as:

$${\rm Slope}=\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$$ An example may make this
definition clearer. If we have two points on a line, $P(x\_1,y\_1)$ and
$Q(x\_2,y\_2)$ , the change in $x$ from $P$ to $Q$ is given by:

$$\Delta x=x\_2-x\_1$$ Likewise, the change in $y$ from $P$ to $Q$ is
given by:

$$\Delta y=y\_2-y\_1$$ This leads to the very important result below.

The slope of the line between the points $(x\_1, y\_1)$ and
    $(x\_2, y\_2)$ is
    $$\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=\frac{y\_2-y\_1}{x\_2-x\_1}$$

Alternatively, we can define slope trigonometrically , using the tangent
function:

$${\rm Slope}=\tan(\alpha)$$ where $\alpha$ is the angle from the
rightward-pointing horizontal to the line, measured counter-clockwise.
If you recall that the tangent of an angle is the ratio of the
y-coordinate to the x-coordinate on the unit circle, you should be able
to spot the equivalence here.

\paragraph{Of a graph of a function}


The graphs of most functions we are interested in are not straight lines
(although they can be), but rather curves. We cannot define the slope of
a curve in the same way as we can for a line. In order for us to
understand how to find the slope of a curve at a point, we will first
have to cover the idea of \textbf{tangency}. Intuitively, a \textbf{tangent} is a
line which \textit{just} touches a curve at a point, such that the angle
between them at that point is 0. Consider the following four curves and
lines:

% [Image: ] % [Image: ] \\% [Image: ] % [Image: ]

1.  The line $L$ crosses, but is not tangent to $C$ at $P$ .
2.  The line $L$ crosses, and is tangent to $C$ at $P$ .
3.  The line $L$ crosses $C$ at two points, but is tangent to $C$ only
    at $P$ .
4.  There are many lines that cross $C$ at $P$, but none are tangent. In
    fact, this curve has no tangent at $P$ .

A \textbf{secant} is a line drawn through two points on a curve. We can
construct a definition of a tangent as the limit of a secant of the
curve taken as the separation between the points tends to zero. Consider
the diagram below.

% [Image: ]

As the distance $h$ tends to 0, the secant line becomes the tangent at
the point $x\_0$ . The two points we draw our line through are:

$$P(x\_0,f(x\_0))$$ and

$$Q(x\_0+h,f(x\_0+h))$$

As a secant line is simply a line and we know two points on it, we can
find its slope, $m\_h$ , using the formula from before:

$$m=\frac{y\_2-y\_1}{x\_2-x\_1}$$

(We will refer to the slope as $m\_h$ because it may, and generally will,
depend on $h$ .) Substituting in the points on the line,

$$m\_h=\frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{(x\_0+h)-x\_0}$$ This simplifies to

$$m\_h=\frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{h}$$ This expression is called the
\textbf{difference quotient}. Note that $h$ can be positive or negative ---
it is perfectly valid to take a secant through any two points on the
curve --- but cannot be $0$ .

The definition of the tangent line we gave was not rigorous, since
we've only defined limits of \textit{numbers} --- or, more precisely, of
functions that output numbers --- not of \textit{lines}. But we \textit{can} define
the \textit{slope} of the tangent line at a point rigorously, by taking the
limit of the slopes of the secant lines from the last paragraph. Having
done so, we can \textit{then} define the tangent line as well. Note that we
cannot simply set $h$ to 0 as this would imply division of 0 by 0 which
would yield an undefined result. Instead we must find the
\href{Calculus/Limits "wikilink"}{\textbf{limit}} of the above expression as $h$
\textbf{tends} to 0:

Definition: Slope of the graph of a function

The \textbf{slope} of the graph of $f(x)$ at the point $(x\_0,f(x\_0))$ is

$\lim\_{h\to 0}\left[\frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{h}\right]$

If this limit does not exist, then we say the slope is \textbf{undefined}.

If the slope is defined, say $m$, then the \textbf{tangent line} to the graph
of $f(x)$ at the point $(x\_0,f(x\_0))$ is the line with equation

$$y-f(x\_0)=m\cdot(x-x\_0)$$

This last equation is just the point-slope form for the line through
$(x\_0,f(x\_0))$ with slope $m$.

\paragraph{The Definition of the Derivative}


You may have noticed that the two operations we've discussed ---
computing the slope of the tangent to the graph of a function and
computing the instantaneous rate of change of the function --- involved
exactly the same limit. That is, the slope of the tangent to the graph
of $y=f(x)$ is $\frac{dy}{dx}$ . Of course, $\frac{dy}{dx}$ can, and
generally will, depend on $x$ , so we should really think of it as a
\textit{function} of $x$ . We call this process (of computing $\frac{dy}{dx}$)
\textbf{differentiation}. Differentiation results in another function whose
value for any value $x$ is the slope of the original function at $x$ .
This function is known as the \textbf{derivative} of the original function.

Since lots of different sorts of people use derivatives, there are lots
of different mathematical notations for them. Here are some:

\begin{itemize}
    \item $f'(x)$ (read "f prime of x") for the derivative of $f(x)$ ,
    \item $D\_x[f(x)]$ ,
    \item $Df(x)$ ,
    \item $\frac{dy}{dx}$ for the derivative of $y$ as a function of $x$ or
    \item $\frac{d}{dx}\big[y\big]$ , which is more useful in some cases.
\end{itemize}

Most of the time the brackets are not needed, but are useful for clarity
if we are dealing with something like $D(f\cdot g)$ , where we want to
differentiate the product of two functions, $f$ and $g$ .

The first notation has the advantage that it makes clear that the
derivative is a function. That is, if we want to talk about the
derivative of $f(x)$ at $x=2$ , we can just write $f'(2)$ .

In any event, here is the formal definition:

Definition: (derivative)

Let $f(x)$ be a function. Then
    $f'(x)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)}{\Delta x}$
    wherever this limit exists. In this case we say that $f$ is
    \textbf{differentiable} at $x$ and its \textbf{derivative} at $x$ is $f'(x)$.

\paragraph{Examples}


\textbf{Example 1}

The derivative of $f(x)=\frac{x}{2}$ is

$f'(x)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\frac{x+\Delta x}{2}-\frac{x}{2}}{\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\frac{x}{2}+\frac{\Delta x}{2}-\frac{x}{2}}{\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\frac{\Delta x}{2}}{\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\Delta x}{2\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac12\right)=\frac12$

no matter what $x$ is. This is consistent with the definition of the
derivative as the slope of a function.

\textbf{Example 2}

What is the slope of the graph of $y=3x^2$ at $(4,48)$ ? We can do it
"the hard (and imprecise) way", \textit{without} using differentiation, as
follows, using a calculator and using small differences below and above
the given point:

When $x=3.999$ , $y=47.976003$ .

When $x=4.001$ , $y=48.024003$ .

Then the difference between the two values of $x$ is $\Delta x=0.002$ .

Then the difference between the two values of $y$ is $\Delta y=0.048$ .

Thus, the slope $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=24$ at the point of the graph
at which $x=4$ .

But, to solve the problem precisely, we compute

$\begin{aligned}
\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{3(4+\Delta x)^2-48}{\Delta x}\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{(4+\Delta x)^2-16}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{16+8\Delta x+(\Delta x)^2-16}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{8\Delta x+(\Delta x)^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}(8+\Delta x) \\\\ \&=3(8) \\\\&=24
\end{aligned}$
  
We were lucky this time; the approximation we got above
turned out to be exactly right. But this won't always be so, and,
anyway, this way we didn't need a calculator.

In general, the derivative of $f(x)=3x^2$ is

$ \begin{aligned}
f'(x)\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{3(x+\Delta x)^2-3x^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{(x+\Delta x)^2-x^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{x^2+2x\Delta x+(\Delta x)^2-x^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{2x\Delta x+(\Delta x)^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}(2x+\Delta x) \\\\&=3(2x) \\\\&=6x
\end{aligned}$

\textbf{Example 3}

If $f(x)=|x|$ (the absolute value function) then $f'(x)=\frac{x}{|x|}$ ,
which can also be stated as

$
f'(x)=
\begin{cases}
-1 \& x0
\end{cases}
$

Finding this derivative is a bit complicated, so we won't prove it at
this point.

Here, $f(x)$ is not smooth (though it is continuous) at $x=0$ and so the
limits $\lim\_{x\to 0^+}f'(x)$ and $\lim\_{x\to 0^-}f'(x)$ (the limits as
0 is approached from the right and left respectively) are not equal.
From the definition,
$f'(0)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{|\Delta x|}{\Delta x}$ , which does not
exist. Thus, $f'(0)$ is undefined, and so $f'(x)$ has a discontinuity at 0. This sort of point of non-differentiability is called a cusp. Functions may also not be differentiable because they go to infinity at
a point, or oscillate infinitely frequently.

\paragraph{Understanding the derivative notation}


The derivative notation is special and unique in mathematics. The most
common notation for derivatives you'll run into when first starting out
with differentiating is the Leibniz notation, expressed as
$\frac{dy}{dx}$ . You may think of this as "rate of change in $y$ with
respect to $x$" . You may also think of it as "infinitesimal value of
$y$ divided by infinitesimal value of $x$" . Either way is a good way
of thinking, although you should remember that the precise definition is
the one we gave above. Often, in an equation, you will see just
$\frac{d}{dx}$ , which literally means "derivative with respect to x".
This means we should take the derivative of whatever is written to the
right; that is, $\frac{d}{dx}(x+2)$ means $\frac{dy}{dx}$ where $y=x+2$
.

As you advance through your studies, you will see that we sometimes
pretend that $dy$ and $dx$ are separate entities that can be multiplied
and divided, by writing things like $dy=x^4dx$ . Eventually you will see
derivatives such as $\frac{dx}{dy}$ , which just means that the input
variable of our function is called $y$ and our output variable is called
$x$ ; sometimes, we will write $\frac{d}{dy}$ , to mean the derivative
with respect to $y$ of whatever is written on the right. In general, the
variables could be anything, say $\frac{d\theta}{dr}$ .

All of the following are equivalent for expressing the derivative of
$y=x^2$

\begin{itemize}
    \item $\frac{dy}{dx}=2x$
    \item $\frac{d}{dx}x^2=2x$
    \item $dy=2x\,dx$
    \item $f'(x)=2x$
    \item $D(f(x))=2x$
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Tangent Lines}


Since we can find the gradient of a function at any point we can also
find the equation of the tangent line to the function at any point:

What is the equation of the tangent line for the curve
$y = x^2 + 3x -16$ at the point x = -4?

First we need to find the derivative of the equation.

$\frac{dy}{dx} = 2x + 3$

Then we find the gradient at the point x = -4.

$2\left ( -4 \right) + 3 = -5$

Using the original function we find the corresponding y value to the x
value at the point of tangency.

$y = -4^2 + 3 \left (-4 \right) -16 = -12$

Then we use the point-gradient equation to find the equation.

$y + 12 = -5 \left ( x + 4 \right )$ so the equation of the tangent line
is y = -5x - 32\....
