\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Define and Calculate the Slope of a Line:} Students will be able to define the concept of slope for a straight line and calculate it using the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x} = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$ given two distinct points on the line. They will also be able to interpret the slope in terms of the line's inclination and how it changes with different values of $\Delta x$ and $\Delta y$.
    \item \textbf{Understand and Apply the Concept of Tangency:} Students will be able to explain the concept of a tangent line and its slope at a given point on a curve. They will learn to find the tangent line by computing the limit of the slope of the secant line as the distance between two points on the curve approaches zero, and apply this concept to find the slope of a function at a specific point using the derivative.
    \item \textbf{Differentiate Functions and Interpret Derivatives:} Students will be able to compute the derivative of a function using the formal definition of the derivative $\frac{dy}{dx} = \lim_{\Delta x \to 0} \frac{f(x + \Delta x) - f(x)}{\Delta x}$. They will also interpret the derivative as the instantaneous rate of change of the function and use it to find the equation of the tangent line at a given point on the function's graph.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Define and Calculate the Slope of a Line}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Given two points on a line, $(3, 4)$ and $(7, 10)$, calculate the slope of the line. Use the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x} = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$ to determine your answer.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What does the slope of a line represent in a coordinate system?
    \item A. The distance between two points on the line.
    \item B. The angle the line makes with the x-axis.
    \item C. The rate at which y changes with respect to x.
    \item D. The y-intercept of the line.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Explain how the slope of a line changes if you increase the value of $\Delta x$ while keeping $\Delta y$ constant. Provide an example with different slopes. \textbf{Part 2: Understand and Apply the Concept of Tangency}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Find the equation of the tangent line to the function $f(x) = x^2 + 2x$ at the point where $x = 1$. First, compute the slope of the tangent line at this point using the derivative.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} The tangent line to a curve at a given point provides the best linear approximation to the curve at that point. (True/False) Provide a brief explanation.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Define the concept of a tangent line to a curve. How is the slope of the tangent line related to the derivative of the function at a given point? \textbf{Part 3: Differentiate Functions and Interpret Derivatives}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Use the definition of the derivative to find the derivative of the function $f(x) = 3x^3 - 2x + 1$. Apply the formula $\frac{dy}{dx} = \lim_{\Delta x \to 0} \frac{f(x + \Delta x) - f(x)}{\Delta x}$ and show your work.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What does the derivative of a function at a specific point represent?
    \item A. The average rate of change of the function over an interval.
    \item B. The instantaneous rate of change of the function at that point.
    \item C. The value of the function at that point.
    \item D. The area under the curve of the function up to that point.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Given the function $g(x) = \sin(x)$, find the derivative at $x = \frac{\pi}{4}$ and explain how the derivative represents the instantaneous rate of change at that point.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{The Derivative as a Function}
\label{sec:the-derivative-as-a-function}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{What is Differentiation?}


Differentiation is a process of finding a function that outputs the
\textbf{rate of change} of one variable with respect to another variable.

Informally, we may suppose that we're tracking the position of a car on
a two-lane road with no passing lanes. Assuming the car never pulls off
the road, we can abstractly study the car's position by assigning it a
variable, $x$ . Since the car's position changes as the time changes,
we say that $x$ is dependent on time, or $x=f(t)$ . This tells where the
car is at each specific time. Differentiation gives us a function
$\frac{dx}{dt}$ which represents the car's speed, that is the rate of
change of its position with respect to time.

Equivalently, differentiation gives us the slope at any point of the
graph of a non-linear function. For a linear function, of form
$f(x)=ax+b$ , $a$ is the slope. For non-linear functions, such as
$f(x)=3x^2$ , the slope can depend on $x$ ; differentiation gives us a
function which represents this slope.

\paragraph{The Definition of Slope}


Historically, the primary motivation for the study of
\textbf{differentiation} was the tangent line problem: for a given curve,
find the slope of the straight line that is tangent to the curve at a
given point. The word \textit{tangent} comes from the Latin word \textit{tangens},
which means touching. Thus, to solve the tangent line problem, we need
to find the slope of a line that is "touching" a given curve at a
given point, or, in modern language, that has the same slope. But what
exactly do we mean by "slope" for a curve?

The solution is obvious in some cases: for example, a line $y=mx+c$ is
its own tangent; the slope at any point is $m$ . For the parabola
$y=x^2$ , the slope at the point $(0,0)$ is $0$ ; the tangent line is
horizontal.

But how can you find the slope of, say, $y=\sin(x)+x^2$ at $x=1.5$ ?
This is in general a nontrivial question, but first we will deal
carefully with the slope of lines.

\paragraph{The Slope of a Line}


The \textbf{slope} of a line, also called the \textbf{gradient} of the line, is a
measure of its inclination. A line that is horizontal has slope 0, a
line from the bottom left to the top right has a positive slope and a
line from the top left to the bottom right has a negative 

% [Image: Three lines with different
slopes]

The slope can be defined in two (equivalent) ways. The first way is to
express it as how much the line climbs for a given motion horizontally.
We denote a change in a quantity using the symbol $\Delta$ (pronounced
"delta"). Thus, a change in $x$ is written as $\Delta x$ . We can
therefore write this definition of slope as:

$${\rm Slope}=\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$$ An example may make this
definition clearer. If we have two points on a line, $P(x\_1,y\_1)$ and
$Q(x\_2,y\_2)$ , the change in $x$ from $P$ to $Q$ is given by:

$$\Delta x=x\_2-x\_1$$ 

Likewise, the change in $y$ from $P$ to $Q$ is given by:

$$\Delta y=y\_2-y\_1$$

This leads to the very important result below.

> 
>
>    The slope of the line between the points $(x\_1, y\_1)$ and
>     $(x\_2, y\_2)$ is
>    $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=\frac{y\_2-y\_1}{x\_2-x\_1}$

Alternatively, we can define slope trigonometrically , using the tangent
function:

$${\rm Slope}=\tan(\alpha)$$ where $\alpha$ is the angle from the
rightward-pointing horizontal to the line, measured counter-clockwise.
If you recall that the tangent of an angle is the ratio of the
y-coordinate to the x-coordinate on the unit circle, you should be able
to spot the equivalence here.

\paragraph{Of a graph of a function}


The graphs of most functions we are interested in are not straight lines
(although they can be), but rather curves. We cannot define the slope of
a curve in the same way as we can for a line. In order for us to
understand how to find the slope of a curve at a point, we will first
have to cover the idea of \textbf{tangency}. Intuitively, a \textbf{tangent} is a
line which \textit{just} touches a curve at a point, such that the angle
between them at that point is 0. Consider the following four curves and
lines:

% [Image: ] % [Image: ] \\% [Image: ] % [Image: ]

1.  The line $L$ crosses, but is not tangent to $C$ at $P$ .
2.  The line $L$ crosses, and is tangent to $C$ at $P$ .
3.  The line $L$ crosses $C$ at two points, but is tangent to $C$ only
    at $P$ .
4.  There are many lines that cross $C$ at $P$, but none are tangent. In
    fact, this curve has no tangent at $P$ .

A \textbf{secant} is a line drawn through two points on a curve. We can
construct a definition of a tangent as the limit of a secant of the
curve taken as the separation between the points tends to zero. Consider
the diagram below.

% [Image: ]

As the distance $h$ tends to 0, the secant line becomes the tangent at
the point $x\_0$ . The two points we draw our line through are:

$$P(x\_0,f(x\_0))$$ and

$$Q(x\_0+h,f(x\_0+h))$$

As a secant line is simply a line and we know two points on it, we can
find its slope, $m\_h$ , using the formula from before:

$$m=\frac{y\_2-y\_1}{x\_2-x\_1}$$

(We will refer to the slope as $m\_h$ because it may, and generally will,
depend on $h$ .) Substituting in the points on the line,

$$m\_h=\frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{(x\_0+h)-x\_0}$$ This simplifies to

$$m\_h=\frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{h}$$ This expression is called the
\textbf{difference quotient}. Note that $h$ can be positive or negative ---
it is perfectly valid to take a secant through any two points on the
curve --- but cannot be $0$ .

The definition of the tangent line we gave was not rigorous, since
we've only defined limits of \textit{numbers} --- or, more precisely, of
functions that output numbers --- not of \textit{lines}. But we \textit{can} define
the \textit{slope} of the tangent line at a point rigorously, by taking the
limit of the slopes of the secant lines from the last paragraph. Having
done so, we can \textit{then} define the tangent line as well. Note that we
cannot simply set $h$ to 0 as this would imply division of 0 by 0 which
would yield an undefined result. Instead we must find the \textbf{limit} of
the above expression as $h$ \textbf{tends} to 0:

> 
>
>    \textbf{Definition: (Slope of the graph of a function)}
>    The \textbf{slope} of the graph of $f(x)$ at the point $(x\_0,f(x\_0))$
>     is
>    $\lim\_{h\to 0}\left[\frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{h}\right]$
>    If this limit does not exist, then we say the slope is
>     \textbf{undefined}.
>    If the slope is defined, say $m$, then the \textbf{tangent line} to the
>     graph of $f(x)$ at the point $(x\_0,f(x\_0))$ is the line with
>     equation
>    $y-f(x\_0)=m\cdot(x-x\_0)$

This last equation is just the point-slope form for the line through
$(x\_0,f(x\_0))$ with slope $m$.

\paragraph{The Rate of Change of a Function at a Point}


Consider the formula for average velocity in the $x$ direction,
$\frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}$ , where $\Delta x$ is the change in $x$ over
the time interval $\Delta t$ . This formula gives the average velocity
over a period of time, but suppose we want to define the instantaneous
velocity. To this end we look at the **change in position as the change
in time approaches 0**. Mathematically this is written as:
$\lim\_{\Delta t\to 0}\frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}$ , which we abbreviate by
the symbol $\frac{dx}{dt}$ . (The idea of this notation is that the
letter $d$ denotes change.) Compare the symbol $d$ with $\Delta$ . The
idea is that both indicate a difference between two numbers, but
$\Delta$ denotes a finite difference while $d$ denotes an infinitesimal
difference. Please note that the symbols $dx$ and $dt$ have no rigorous
meaning on their own, since $\lim\_{\Delta t\to 0}\Delta t=0$ , and we
can't divide by 0.

(Note that the letter $s$ is often used to denote distance, which would
yield $\frac{ds}{dt}$ . The letter $d$ is often avoided in denoting
distance due to the potential confusion resulting from the expression
$\frac{dd}{dt}$.)

\paragraph{The Definition of the Derivative}


You may have noticed that the two operations we've discussed ---
computing the slope of the tangent to the graph of a function and
computing the instantaneous rate of change of the function --- involved
exactly the same limit. That is, the slope of the tangent to the graph
of $y=f(x)$ is $\frac{dy}{dx}$ . Of course, $\frac{dy}{dx}$ can, and
generally will, depend on $x$ , so we should really think of it as a
\textit{function} of $x$ . We call this process (of computing $\frac{dy}{dx}$)
\textbf{differentiation}. Differentiation results in another function whose
value for any value $x$ is the slope of the original function at $x$ .
This function is known as the \textbf{derivative} of the original function.

Since lots of different sorts of people use derivatives, there are lots
of different mathematical notations for them. Here are some:

\begin{itemize}
    \item $f'(x)$ (read "f prime of x") for the derivative of $f(x)$ ,
    \item $D\_x[f(x)]$ ,
    \item $Df(x)$ ,
    \item $\frac{dy}{dx}$ for the derivative of $y$ as a function of $x$ or
    \item $\frac{d}{dx}\big[y\big]$ , which is more useful in some cases.
\end{itemize}

Most of the time the brackets are not needed, but are useful for clarity
if we are dealing with something like $D(f\cdot g)$ , where we want to
differentiate the product of two functions, $f$ and $g$ .

The first notation has the advantage that it makes clear that the
derivative is a function. That is, if we want to talk about the
derivative of $f(x)$ at $x=2$ , we can just write $f'(2)$ .

In any event, here is the formal definition:

> 
>
>    \textbf{Definition: (derivative)}
>    Let $f(x)$ be a function. Then
>     $f'(x)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)}{\Delta x}$
>     wherever this limit exists. In this case we say that $f$ is
>     \textbf{differentiable} at $x$ and its \textbf{derivative} at $x$ is $f'(x)$
>     .

\paragraph{Examples}


\textbf{Example 1}

The derivative of $f(x)=\frac{x}{2}$ is

$$f'(x)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\frac{x+\Delta x}{2}-\frac{x}{2}}{\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\frac{x}{2}+\frac{\Delta x}{2}-\frac{x}{2}}{\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\frac{\Delta x}{2}}{\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac{\Delta x}{2\Delta x}\right)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left(\frac12\right)=\frac12$$
no matter what $x$ is. This is consistent with the definition of the
derivative as the slope of a function.

\textbf{Example 2}

What is the slope of the graph of $y=3x^2$ at $(4,48)$ ? We can do it
"the hard (and imprecise) way", \textit{without} using differentiation, as
follows, using a calculator and using small differences below and above
the given point:

When $x=3.999$ , $y=47.976003$ .

When $x=4.001$ , $y=48.024003$ .

Then the difference between the two values of $x$ is $\Delta x=0.002$ .

Then the difference between the two values of $y$ is $\Delta y=0.048$ .

Thus, the slope $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=24$ at the point of the graph
at which $x=4$ .

But, to solve the problem precisely, we compute

$\begin{aligned}
\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{3(4+\Delta x)^2-48}{\Delta x}\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{(4+\Delta x)^2-16}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{16+8\Delta x+(\Delta x)^2-16}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{8\Delta x+(\Delta x)^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&=3\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}(8+\Delta x) \\\\&=3(8) \\\\&=24
\end{aligned}$

We were lucky this time; the approximation we got above
turned out to be exactly right. But this won't always be so, and,
anyway, this way we didn't need a calculator.

In general, the derivative of $f(x)=3x^2$ is

$\begin{aligned}
f'(x) \&= \lim _{\Delta x \to 0}\frac{3(x+\Delta x)^2-3x^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&= 3\lim _{\Delta x \to 0}\frac{(x+\Delta x)^2 - x^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&= 3\lim _{\Delta x \to 0}\frac{x^2 + 2x\Delta x + (\Delta x)^2 - x^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&= 3\lim _{\Delta x \to 0}\frac{2x\Delta x + (\Delta x)^2}{\Delta x} \\\\&= 3\lim _{\Delta x \to 0}(2x + \Delta x) \\\\&= 3(2x) \\\\&= 6x \end{aligned}$

\textbf{Example 3}

If $f(x)=|x|$ (the absolute value function) then $f'(x)=\frac{x}{|x|}$ ,
which can also be stated as

$f'(x)=
\begin{cases}-1\&x0
\end{cases}$

Finding this derivative is a bit complicated, so we won't prove it at
this point.

Here, $f(x)$ is not smooth (though it is continuous) at $x=0$ and so the
limits $\lim\_{x\to 0^+}f'(x)$ and $\lim\_{x\to 0^-}f'(x)$ (the limits as
0 is approached from the right and left respectively) are not equal.
From the definition,
$f'(0)=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{|\Delta x|}{\Delta x}$ , which does not
exist. Thus, $f'(0)$ is undefined, and so $f'(x)$ has a discontinuity at
0. This sort of point of non-differentiability is called a cusp.
Functions may also not be differentiable because they go to infinity at
a point, or oscillate infinitely frequently.

\paragraph{Understanding the derivative notation}


The derivative notation is special and unique in mathematics. The most
common notation for derivatives you'll run into when first starting out
with differentiating is the Leibniz notation, expressed as
$\frac{dy}{dx}$ . You may think of this as "rate of change in $y$ with
respect to $x$" . You may also think of it as "infinitesimal value of
$y$ divided by infinitesimal value of $x$" . Either way is a good way
of thinking, although you should remember that the precise definition is
the one we gave above. Often, in an equation, you will see just
$\frac{d}{dx}$ , which literally means "derivative with respect to x".
This means we should take the derivative of whatever is written to the
right; that is, $\frac{d}{dx}(x+2)$ means $\frac{dy}{dx}$ where $y=x+2$
.

As you advance through your studies, you will see that we sometimes
pretend that $dy$ and $dx$ are separate entities that can be multiplied
and divided, by writing things like $dy=x^4dx$ . Eventually you will see
derivatives such as $\frac{dx}{dy}$ , which just means that the input
variable of our function is called $y$ and our output variable is called
$x$ ; sometimes, we will write $\frac{d}{dy}$ , to mean the derivative
with respect to $y$ of whatever is written on the right. In general, the
variables could be anything, say $\frac{d\theta}{dr}$ .

All of the following are equivalent for expressing the derivative of
$y=x^2$

\begin{itemize}
    \item $\frac{dy}{dx}=2x$
    \item $\frac{d}{dx}x^2=2x$
    \item $dy=2x\,dx$
    \item $f'(x)=2x$
    \item $D(f(x))=2x$
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Differentiation Rules}


The process of differentiation is tedious for complicated functions.
Therefore, rules for differentiating general functions have been
developed, and can be proved with a little effort. Once sufficient rules
have been proved, it will be fairly easy to differentiate a wide variety
of functions. Some of the simplest rules involve the derivative of
linear functions.

\paragraph{Derivative of a constant function}


For any fixed real number $c$ ,



$$\frac{d}{dx}[c]=0$$



\paragraph{Intuition}


The graph of the function $f(x)=c$ is a horizontal line, which has a
constant slope of 0. Therefore, it should be expected that the
derivative of this function is zero, regardless of the values of $x$ and
$c$ .

\paragraph{Proof}


The definition of a derivative is

$$\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)}{\Delta x}$$ Let $f(x)=c$
for all $x$ . (That is, $f$ is a constant function.) Then
$f(x+\Delta x)=c$ . Therefore

$$\frac{d}{dx}[c]=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{c-c}{\Delta x}=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{0}{\Delta x}$$

Let $g(\Delta x)=\frac{0}{\Delta x}$ . To prove that
$\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}g(\Delta x)=0$ , we need to find a positive
$\delta$ such that, for any given positive $\varepsilon$ ,
$\Big|g(\Delta x)-0\Big|<\varepsilon$ whenever $0<|\Delta x-0|<\delta$ .
But $\Big|g(\Delta x)-0\Big|=0$ , so
$\Big|g(\Delta x)-0\Big|<\varepsilon$ for any choice of $\delta$ .

\paragraph{Examples}


1.  $\frac{d}{dx}[3]=0$
2.  $\frac{d}{dx}[z]=0$

Note that, in the second example, $z$ is just a constant.

\paragraph{Derivative of a linear function}


For any fixed real numbers $m$ and $c$ ,



$$\frac{d}{dx}[mx+c]=m$$



The special case $\frac{dx}{dx}=1$ shows the advantage of the
$\frac{d}{dx}$ notation---rules are intuitive by basic algebra, though
this does not constitute a proof, and can lead to misconceptions to what
exactly $dx$ and $dy$ actually are.

\paragraph{Intuition}


The graph of $y=mx+c$ is a line with constant slope $m$.

\paragraph{Proof}


If $f(x)=mx+c$ , then $f(x+\Delta x)=m(x+\Delta x)+c$. So,

$\begin{aligned}
f'(x)\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{m(x+\Delta x)+c-mx-c}{\Delta x} \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{m(x+\Delta x)-mx}{\Delta x} \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{mx+m\Delta x-mx}{\Delta x} \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{m\Delta x}{\Delta x} \\\\&=m
\end{aligned}$

\paragraph{Constant multiple and addition rules}


Since we already know the rules for some very basic functions, we would
like to be able to take the derivative of more complex functions by
breaking them up into simpler functions. Two tools that let us do this
are the constant multiple rule and the addition rule.

\paragraph{The Constant Rule}


For any fixed real number $c$ ,



$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[c\cdot f(x)\big]=c\cdot\frac{d}{dx}\big[f(x)\big]$$



The reason, of course, is that one can factor $c$ out of the numerator,
and then of the entire limit, in the definition.

\textbf{Example}

We already know that

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^2\big]=2x$$ Suppose we want to find the derivative
of $3x^2$

$\begin{aligned}
\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big]\&=3\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^2\big] \\\\&=3\cdot2x \\\\&=6x
\end{aligned}$

Another simple rule for breaking up functions is the addition rule.

\paragraph{The Addition and Subtraction Rules}




$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[f(x)\pm g(x)\big]=\frac{d}{dx}\big[f(x)\big]\pm\frac{d}{dx}\big[g(x)\big]$$



\textbf{Proof}

From the definition:

$\begin{aligned}
\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{\big[f(x+\Delta x)\pm g(x+\Delta x)\big]-\big[f(x)\pm g(x)\big]}{\Delta x}\right]\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{\big[f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)\big]\pm\big[g(x+\Delta x)-g(x)\big]}{\Delta x}\right] \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)}{\Delta x}\right]\pm\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{g(x+\Delta x)-g(x)}{\Delta x}\right]
\end{aligned}$

By definition then, this last term is
$\frac{d}{dx} \left[f(x)\right] \pm \frac{d}{dx}\left[g(x)\right]$

\textbf{Example}

What is the derivative of $3x^2+5x$ ?

> $
\begin{aligned}
\&\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2+5x\big] \\\\&\quad = \frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2+5x\big] \\\\&\quad = \frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big] + \frac{d}{dx}\big[5x\big] \\\\&\quad = 6x + \frac{d}{dx}\big[5x\big] \\\\&\quad = 6x + 5 \end{aligned}
$


The fact that both of these rules work is extremely significant
mathematically because it means that differentiation is \textbf{linear}. You
can take an equation, break it up into terms, figure out the derivative
individually and build the answer back up, and nothing odd will happen.

We now need only one more piece of information before we can take the
derivatives of any polynomial.

\paragraph{The Power Rule}




$\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^n\big]=nx^{n-1}$



This has been proved in an example in Derivatives of Exponential and
Logarithm Functions where it can be best understood.

For example, in the case of $x^2$ the derivative is $2x^1=2x$ as was
established earlier. A special case of this rule is that
$\frac{dx}{dx}=\frac{dx^1}{dx}=1x^0=1$ .

Since polynomials are sums of monomials, using this rule and the
addition rule lets you differentiate any polynomial. A relatively simple
proof for this can be derived from the binomial expansion theorem.

This rule also applies to fractional and negative powers. Therefore

$\begin{aligned}
\frac{d}{dx}\big[\sqrt x\big]\&=\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^\frac12\big] \\\\&=\frac12x^{-\frac12} \\\\&=\frac{1}{2\sqrt x}
\end{aligned}$

\paragraph{Derivatives of polynomials}


With these rules in hand, you can now find the derivative of any
polynomial you come across. Rather than write the general formula,
let's go step by step through the process.

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[6x^5+3x^2+3x+1\big]$$ The first thing we can do is to
use the addition rule to split the equation up into terms:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[6x^5\big]+\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big]+\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x\big]+\frac{d}{dx}[1]$$

We can immediately use the linear and constant rules to get rid of some
terms:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[6x^5\big]+\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big]+3+0$$ Now you
may use the constant multiplier rule to move the constants outside the
derivatives:

$$6\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^5\big]+3\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^2\big]+3$$ Then use the
power rule to work with the individual monomials:

$$6(5x^4)+3(2x)+3$$ And then do some algebra to get the final answer:

$$30x^4+6x+3$$ These are not the only differentiation rules. There are
other, more advanced, differentiation rules, which will be described in
a later chapter.

\end{itemize}
    Function}

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Understand the Concept of Slope and Differentiation:} Students will be able to explain the concept of slope in both linear and non-linear contexts, and describe how differentiation provides the rate of change of a function with respect to its variable. They will identify and describe the relationship between the slope of a tangent line and the derivative of a function at a given point.
    \item \textbf{Compute the Slope of a Line and the Derivative of Simple Functions:} Students will be able to calculate the slope of a line given two points using the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$, and apply the differentiation rules to find the derivative of basic functions, such as constant functions, linear functions, and quadratic functions. They will also be able to use the definition of the derivative to find the instantaneous rate of change for these functions.
    \item \textbf{Apply the Definition of the Derivative to Find the Slope of a Tangent Line:} Students will be able to use the limit definition of the derivative to find the slope of the tangent line to the graph of a function at a given point. They will demonstrate this by solving problems involving the difference quotient and interpreting the results in terms of instantaneous rate of change and the slope of the tangent line.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Understand the Concept of Slope and Differentiation}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Explain the concept of slope for a straight line and for a curve. How does the slope of a tangent line at a point on a curve relate to the derivative of the function at that point?
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following best describes the role of differentiation in relation to a function's rate of change?
    \item A. Differentiation provides the area under the curve of the function.
    \item B. Differentiation measures the total accumulated change of the function over an interval.
    \item C. Differentiation gives the instantaneous rate of change of the function at any given point.
    \item D. Differentiation calculates the function's value at a specific point.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} The slope of the tangent line to the curve of a function at a given point is the same as the average rate of change over a small interval around that point. (True/False) Explain why this statement is true or false. \textbf{Part 2: Compute the Slope of a Line and the Derivative of Simple Functions}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Given the points $(2, 3)$ and $(5, 7)$, calculate the slope of the line connecting these points using the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x} = \frac{y_2 - y_1}{x_2 - x_1}$.
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Find the derivative of the function $f(x) = 4x^2 - 3x + 2$ using basic differentiation rules. Show your work.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the definition of the derivative to find the instantaneous rate of change of the function $g(x) = x^2 + 5x$ at $x = 2$. Apply the limit definition $\frac{dy}{dx} = \lim_{\Delta x \to 0} \frac{f(x + \Delta x) - f(x)}{\Delta x}$ and explain your result. \textbf{Part 3: Apply the Definition of the Derivative to Find the Slope of a Tangent Line}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Compute the derivative of the function $h(x) = 3x^3 - 2x + 1$ at the point $x = 1$ using the limit definition of the derivative: $\frac{dy}{dx} = \lim_{\Delta x \to 0} \frac{h(x + \Delta x) - h(x)}{\Delta x}$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What does the limit $\lim_{\Delta x \to 0} \frac{f(x + \Delta x) - f(x)}{\Delta x}$ represent for a function $f(x)$ at a point $x$?
    \item A. The total change in $f(x)$ over the interval $[x, x + \Delta x]$.
    \item B. The average rate of change of $f(x)$ over the interval $[x, x + \Delta x]$.
    \item C. The instantaneous rate of change of $f(x)$ at the point $x$.
    \item D. The maximum value of $f(x)$ over the interval $[x, x + \Delta x]$.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the limit definition to find the slope of the tangent line to the curve of the function $f(x) = x^3 - 3x^2 + 2x$ at $x = 0$. Interpret the result in terms of the slope of the tangent line.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Differentiation Rules}
\label{sec:differentiation-rules}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{Differentiation Rules}


The process of differentiation is tedious for complicated functions.
Therefore, rules for differentiating general functions have been
developed, and can be proved with a little effort. Once sufficient rules
have been proved, it will be fairly easy to differentiate a wide variety
of functions. Some of the simplest rules involve the derivative of
linear functions.

\paragraph{Derivative of a constant function}


For any fixed real number $c$ ,



$$\frac{d}{dx}[c]=0$$



\paragraph{Intuition}


The graph of the function $f(x)=c$ is a horizontal line, which has a
constant slope of 0. Therefore, it should be expected that the
derivative of this function is zero, regardless of the values of $x$ and
$c$ .

\paragraph{Proof}


The definition of a derivative is

$$\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)}{\Delta x}$$ Let $f(x)=c$
for all $x$ . (That is, $f$ is a constant function.) Then
$f(x+\Delta x)=c$ . Therefore

$$\frac{d}{dx}[c]=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{c-c}{\Delta x}=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{0}{\Delta x}$$

Let $g(\Delta x)=\frac{0}{\Delta x}$ . To prove that
$\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}g(\Delta x)=0$ , we need to find a positive
$\delta$ such that, for any given positive $\varepsilon$ ,
$\Big|g(\Delta x)-0\Big|<\varepsilon$ whenever $0<|\Delta x-0|<\delta$ .
But $\Big|g(\Delta x)-0\Big|=0$ , so
$\Big|g(\Delta x)-0\Big|<\varepsilon$ for any choice of $\delta$ .

\paragraph{Examples}


1.  $\frac{d}{dx}[3]=0$
2.  $\frac{d}{dx}[z]=0$

Note that, in the second example, $z$ is just a constant.

\paragraph{Derivative of a linear function}


For any fixed real numbers $m$ and $c$ ,



$$\frac{d}{dx}[mx+c]=m$$



The special case $\frac{dx}{dx}=1$ shows the advantage of the
$\frac{d}{dx}$ notation---rules are intuitive by basic algebra, though
this does not constitute a proof, and can lead to misconceptions to what
exactly $dx$ and $dy$ actually are.

\paragraph{Intuition}


The graph of $y=mx+c$ is a line with constant slope $m$.

\paragraph{Proof}


If $f(x)=mx+c$ , then $f(x+\Delta x)=m(x+\Delta x)+c$. So,

$\begin{aligned}
f'(x)\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{m(x+\Delta x)+c-mx-c}{\Delta x} \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{m(x+\Delta x)-mx}{\Delta x} \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{mx+m\Delta x-mx}{\Delta x} \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\frac{m\Delta x}{\Delta x} \\\\&=m
\end{aligned}$

\paragraph{Constant multiple and addition rules}


Since we already know the rules for some very basic functions, we would
like to be able to take the derivative of more complex functions by
breaking them up into simpler functions. Two tools that let us do this
are the constant multiple rule and the addition rule.

\paragraph{The Constant Rule}


For any fixed real number $c$ ,



$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[c\cdot f(x)\big]=c\cdot\frac{d}{dx}\big[f(x)\big]$$



The reason, of course, is that one can factor $c$ out of the numerator,
and then of the entire limit, in the definition.

\textbf{Example}

We already know that

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^2\big]=2x$$ Suppose we want to find the derivative
of $3x^2$

$\begin{aligned}
\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big]\&=3\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^2\big] \\\\&=3\cdot2x \\\\&=6x
\end{aligned}$

Another simple rule for breaking up functions is the addition rule.

\paragraph{The Addition and Subtraction Rules}




$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[f(x)\pm g(x)\big]=\frac{d}{dx}\big[f(x)\big]\pm\frac{d}{dx}\big[g(x)\big]$$



\textbf{Proof}

From the definition:

$\begin{aligned}
\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{\big[f(x+\Delta x)\pm g(x+\Delta x)\big]-\big[f(x)\pm g(x)\big]}{\Delta x}\right]\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{\big[f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)\big]\pm\big[g(x+\Delta x)-g(x)\big]}{\Delta x}\right] \\\\&=\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{f(x+\Delta x)-f(x)}{\Delta x}\right]\pm\lim\_{\Delta x\to 0}\left[\frac{g(x+\Delta x)-g(x)}{\Delta x}\right]
\end{aligned}$

By definition then, this last term is
$\frac{d}{dx} \left[f(x)\right] \pm \frac{d}{dx}\left[g(x)\right]$

\textbf{Example}

What is the derivative of $3x^2+5x$ ?

> $
\begin{aligned}
\&\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2+5x\big] \\\\&\quad = \frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2+5x\big] \\\\&\quad = \frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big] + \frac{d}{dx}\big[5x\big] \\\\&\quad = 6x + \frac{d}{dx}\big[5x\big] \\\\&\quad = 6x + 5 \end{aligned}
$


The fact that both of these rules work is extremely significant
mathematically because it means that differentiation is \textbf{linear}. You
can take an equation, break it up into terms, figure out the derivative
individually and build the answer back up, and nothing odd will happen.

We now need only one more piece of information before we can take the
derivatives of any polynomial.

\paragraph{The Power Rule}




$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^n\big]=nx^{n-1}$$



This has been proved in an example in Derivatives of Exponential and
Logarithm Functions where it can be best understood.

For example, in the case of $x^2$ the derivative is $2x^1=2x$ as was
established earlier. A special case of this rule is that
$\frac{dx}{dx}=\frac{dx^1}{dx}=1x^0=1$ .

Since polynomials are sums of monomials, using this rule and the
addition rule lets you differentiate any polynomial. A relatively simple
proof for this can be derived from the binomial expansion theorem.

This rule also applies to fractional and negative powers. Therefore

$\begin{aligned}
\frac{d}{dx}\big[\sqrt x\big]\&=\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^\frac12\big] \\\\&=\frac12x^{-\frac12} \\\\&=\frac{1}{2\sqrt x}
\end{aligned}$

\paragraph{Derivatives of polynomials}


With these rules in hand, you can now find the derivative of any
polynomial you come across. Rather than write the general formula,
let's go step by step through the process.

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[6x^5+3x^2+3x+1\big]$$ The first thing we can do is to
use the addition rule to split the equation up into terms:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[6x^5\big]+\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big]+\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x\big]+\frac{d}{dx}[1]$$

We can immediately use the linear and constant rules to get rid of some
terms:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\big[6x^5\big]+\frac{d}{dx}\big[3x^2\big]+3+0$$ Now you
may use the constant multiplier rule to move the constants outside the
derivatives:

$$6\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^5\big]+3\frac{d}{dx}\big[x^2\big]+3$$ Then use the
power rule to work with the individual monomials:

$$6(5x^4)+3(2x)+3$$ And then do some algebra to get the final answer:

$$30x^4+6x+3$$ These are not the only differentiation rules. There are
other, more advanced, differentiation rules, which will be described in
a later chapter.

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Apply Differentiation Rules:} Students will be able to apply differentiation rules to find the derivative of constant functions and linear functions, including functions of the form $mx + c$.
    \item \textbf{Apply Constant Multiple and Addition Rules:} Students will be able to utilize the constant multiple rule and addition rule to differentiate composite functions by breaking them into simpler components.
    \item *Apply the Power Rule:** Students will be able to use the power rule to determine the derivatives of polynomial functions, including those with fractional and negative exponents.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Apply Differentiation Rules}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Find the derivative of the function $f(x) = 7$ using the constant function rule.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Compute the derivative of the function$$g(x) = 5x - 3$$. Describe the role of the differentiation rules applied in this process.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which rule should be used to differentiate the function $h(x) = 3x + 4$?
    \item A. Product Rule
    \item B. Quotient Rule
    \item C. Constant Rule
    \item D. Sum Rule \textbf{Part 2: Apply Constant Multiple and Addition Rules}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Differentiate the function $f(x) = 6x^2 + 4x - 2$ using the constant multiple and addition rules.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Explain how to use the constant multiple rule to differentiate the function $g(x) = 7 \cdot (2x^3 - 4x)$. Provide the derivative.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} The derivative of the sum of two functions is equal to the sum of their derivatives. (True/False) Provide a brief explanation to support your answer. \textbf{Part 3: Apply the Power Rule}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Find the derivative of the polynomial function $f(x) = 3x^4 - 5x^2 + 2x - 7$ using the power rule.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the power rule to differentiate the function $g(x) = x^{1/2} + x^{-3}$. Explain how the power rule applies to fractional and negative exponents.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What is the derivative of $f(x) = x^{-2}$ according to the power rule?
    \item A. $-2x^{-3}$
    \item B. $2x^{-3}$
    \item C. $-x^{-1}$
    \item D. $x^{-2}$
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Derivatives as Rates of Change}
\label{sec:derivatives-as-rates-of-change}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{Average Rate of Change}


Rate of Change is used to describe the ratio of a change in one variable
compare to a change of another variable

If there is a function $f(x)=y$ then the rate of change of the function
$f(x)$ correspond to the rate of change of variable x is

$$\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=\frac{\Delta f(x)}{\Delta x}$$

For example, if there are two points $(1,3)$ and $(2,7),$ then

$$\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=\frac{\Delta f(x)}{\Delta x}=\frac{7-3}{2-1}=4$$

\paragraph{The Rate of Change of a Function at a Point}


Consider the formula for average velocity in the $x$ direction,
$\frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}$ , where $\Delta x$ is the change in $x$ over
the time interval $\Delta t$ . This formula gives the average velocity
over a period of time, but suppose we want to define the instantaneous
velocity. To this end we look at the **change in position as the change
in time approaches 0**. Mathematically this is written as:
$\lim\_{\Delta t\to 0}\frac{\Delta x}{\Delta t}$ , which we abbreviate by
the symbol $\frac{dx}{dt}$ . (The idea of this notation is that the
letter $d$ denotes change.) Compare the symbol $d$ with $\Delta$ . The
idea is that both indicate a difference between two numbers, but
$\Delta$ denotes a finite difference while $d$ denotes an infinitesimal
difference. Please note that the symbols $dx$ and $dt$ have no rigorous
meaning on their own, since $\lim\_{\Delta t\to 0}\Delta t=0$ , and we
can't divide by 0.

(Note that the letter $s$ is often used to denote distance, which would
yield $\frac{ds}{dt}$ . The letter $d$ is often avoided in denoting
distance due to the potential confusion resulting from the expression
$\frac{dd}{dt}.$)

\paragraph{Definition of Instantaneous Rate of Change (Derivative)}


Let $f(x)$ be a function. Then
$f'(x)=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{f(x+h)-f(x)}{h}$ wherever this limit exists.
In this case we say that $f$ is \textbf{differentiable} at $x$ and its
\textbf{derivative} at $x$ is $f'(x).$

% [Image: ]

Think of $h$ as being the distance between the points $(x\_0, f(x\_0))$
and $(x\_0 + h, f(x\_0 + h)).$ Then, the average rate of change between
these two points is

$\frac{\Delta f(x)}{\Delta x} = \frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{(x\_0 + h) - x\_0} = \frac{f(x\_0+h)-f(x\_0)}{h}.$

As the distance between the points goes to 0, we approach the
instantaneous rate of change (slope) of $f(x)$ at the point $x = x\_0.$
This is how we derive (no pun intended) the definition of the
derivative.

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Calculate the Average Rate of Change:} Students will be able to compute the average rate of change of a function ($f(x)$) between two given points by applying the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x} = \frac{f(x\_2) - f(x\_1)}{x\_2 - x\_1}$, where $ (x\_1, y\_1) $ and $ (x\_2, y\_2) $ are the given points.
    \item \textbf{Understand and Explain the Concept of Instantaneous Rate of Change:} Students will be able to explain the concept of instantaneous rate of change (derivative) as the limit of the average rate of change as the interval between points approaches zero, using the definition \( $f'(x) = \lim\_{h \to 0} \frac{f(x+h) - f(x)}{h} $.
    \item \textbf{Differentiate Between Average and Instantaneous Rate of Change:} Students will be able to distinguish between average rate of change and instantaneous rate of change by comparing the finite difference formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$ with the infinitesimal difference formula $\frac{dy}{dx}$ and discussing their applications in different contexts.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Calculate the Average Rate of Change}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Compute the average rate of change of the function $f(x) = 3x^2 + 2x$ between the points $x_1 = 1$ and $x_2 = 4$ using the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x} = \frac{f(x_2) - f(x_1)}{x_2 - x_1}$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} For the function $f(x) = \sqrt{x}$, what is the average rate of change between $x = 2$ and $x = 5$?
    \item A. $\frac{\sqrt{5} - \sqrt{2}}{3}$
    \item B. $\frac{\sqrt{5} - \sqrt{2}}{5 - 2}$
    \item C. $\frac{5 - 2}{\sqrt{5} - \sqrt{2}}$
    \item D.$$\frac{\sqrt{2} - \sqrt{5}}{2 - 5}$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find the average rate of change of the function $f(x) = x^3 - 4x$ between $x = -1$ and $x = 2$. Show your calculations. \textbf{Part 2: Understand and Explain the Concept of Instantaneous Rate of Change}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Use the definition of the derivative to find the instantaneous rate of change of the function $f(x) = x^2 + 3x$ at $x = 2$. Apply the limit definition $f'(x) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f(x+h) - f(x)}{h}$.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Explain in your own words how the instantaneous rate of change at a point on the function $f(x) = \sin(x)$ is different from the average rate of change over an interval. Use the limit definition $f'(x) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f(x+h) - f(x)}{h}$ in your explanation.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} The instantaneous rate of change of a function at a point is the same as the average rate of change over an infinitesimally small interval around that point. (True/False) Provide a brief explanation for your answer. \textbf{Part 3: Differentiate Between Average and Instantaneous Rate of Change}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Describe how the formula $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$ is used to calculate the average rate of change and compare it with $\frac{dy}{dx}$, the derivative, which gives the instantaneous rate of change. Include a brief example illustrating both concepts.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following statements correctly distinguishes between average rate of change and instantaneous rate of change?
    \item A. The average rate of change is calculated over a finite interval, while the instantaneous rate of change is calculated over an infinitesimally small interval.
    \item B. The instantaneous rate of change is calculated over a finite interval, while the average rate of change is calculated over an infinitesimally small interval.
    \item C. The average rate of change measures the slope of the tangent line, while the instantaneous rate of change measures the slope of the secant line.
    \item D. The average rate of change is used to find the derivative, while the instantaneous rate of change is used to find the integral.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Given the function $f(x) = x^2 + 2x$, compute both the average rate of change over the interval from $x = 1$ to $x = 4$ and the instantaneous rate of change at $x = 2$. Explain the difference between the two rates of change.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Derivatives of the Trigonometric Functions}
\label{sec:derivatives-of-the-trigonometric-functions}
%%===============================================================

Sine, cosine, tangent, cosecant, secant, cotangent. These are functions
that crop up continuously in mathematics and engineering and have a lot
of practical applications. They also appear in more advanced
mathematics, particularly when dealing with things such as line
integrals with complex numbers and alternate representations of space
like spherical and cylindrical coordinate systems.

We use the definition of the derivative, i.e.,

$$f'(x)=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{f(x+h)-f(x)}{h},$$

to work these first two out.

Let us find the derivative of sin(\textit{x}), using the above definition.

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=\sin(x) \\\f'(x)&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\sin(x+h)-\sin(x)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{Definition of derivative} \\\&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x)\sin(h)+\cos(h)\sin(x)-\sin(x)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{trigonometric identity} \\\&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x)\sin(h)+(\cos(h)-1)\sin(x)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{factoring} \\\&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x)\sin(h)}{h}+\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{(\cos(h)-1)\sin(x)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{separation of terms} \\\\ 
&=\cos(x)\times 1+\sin(x)\times 0 \hspace{1cm} \text{application of limit} \\\&=\cos(x) \hspace{1cm} solution
\end{aligned}$$

Now for the case of cos(\textit{x}).

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=\cos(x) \\\f'(x)&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x+h)-\cos(x)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{Definition of derivative} \\\&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x)\cos(h)-\sin(h)\sin(x)-\cos(x)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{trigonometric identity} \\\&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x)(\cos(h)-1)-\sin(x)\sin(h)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{factoring} \\\&=\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\cos(x)(\cos(h)-1)}{h}-\lim\_{h\to 0}\frac{\sin(x)\sin(h)}{h} \hspace{1cm} \text{separation of terms} \\\\ 
&=\cos(x)\times 0-\sin(x)\times 1$ \hspace{1cm} \text{application of limit} \\\&=-\sin(x) \hspace{1cm} solution
\end{aligned}$$

Therefore we have established

 \textbf{Derivative of Sine and Cosine} 
$$\frac{d}{dx}\sin(x)=\cos(x)$$ 
$$\frac{d}{dx}\cos(x)=-\sin(x)$$ 

To find the derivative of the tangent, we just remember that:

$$\tan(x)=\frac{\sin(x)}{\cos(x)}$$

which is a quotient. Applying the quotient rule, we get:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\tan(x)=\frac{\cos^2(x)+\sin^2(x)}{\cos^2(x)}$$

Then, remembering that $\cos^2(x)+\sin^2(x)=1$ , we simplify:

$$\begin{aligned}
\frac{\cos^2(x)+\sin^2(x)}{\cos^2(x)}&=\frac{1}{\cos^2(x)} \\\&=\sec^2(x)
\end{aligned}$$

 \textbf{Derivative of the Tangent}
 $$\frac{d}{dx}\tan(x)=\sec^2(x)$$

For secants, we again apply the quotient rule.

$$\sec(x)=\frac{1}{\cos(x)}$$

$$\begin{aligned}\frac{d}{dx}\sec(x)&=\frac{d}{dx}\frac{1}{\cos(x)} \\\&=\frac{\cos(x)\frac{d 1}{dx}-1\frac{d\cos(x)}{dx}}{\cos(x)^2} \\\&=\frac{\cos(x)0-1(-\sin(x))}{\cos(x)^2}
\end{alignd}$$

Leaving us with:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\sec(x)=\frac{\sin(x)}{\cos^2(x)}$$

Simplifying, we get:

 \textbf{Derivative of the Secant}        
$$\frac{d}{dx}\sec(x)=\sec(x)\tan(x)$$ 

Using the same procedure on cosecants:

$$\csc(x)=\frac{1}{\sin(x)}$$

We get:

 \textbf{Derivative of the Cosecant}        
 $$\frac{d}{dx}\csc(x)=-\csc(x)\cot(x)$$ 


Using the same procedure for the cotangent that we used for the tangent,
we get:

 \textbf{Derivative of the Cotangent}
 $$\frac{d}{dx}\cot(x)=-\csc^2(x)$$

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Compute the Derivatives of Basic Trigonometric Functions:} Students will be able to find the derivatives of the basic trigonometric functions: sine $(\sin(x))$, cosine $(\cos(x))$, and tangent $(\tan(x))$, using the limit definition of the derivative and trigonometric identities.
    \item \textbf{Apply the Quotient Rule to Derivatives of Trigonometric Functions:} Students will be able to apply the quotient rule to find the derivatives of trigonometric functions expressed as quotients, such as secant $(\sec(x))$ and cosecant $(\csc(x))$.
    \item \textbf{Differentiate Between Trigonometric Functions and Their Derivatives:} Students will be able to explain the relationships between the trigonometric functions and their derivatives and how these derivatives are used in different applications. They should understand the significance of each derivative and be able to apply these derivatives to solve problems involving rates of change in trigonometric contexts.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Compute the Derivatives of Basic Trigonometric Functions}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Find the derivative of the function $f(x) = \sin(x)$ using the limit definition of the derivative. Show your work and include the final result.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What is the derivative of the function $g(x) = \cos(x)$?
    \item A. $-\sin(x)$
    \item B. $\sin(x)$
    \item C. $-\cos(x)$
    \item D. $\cos(x)$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Compute the derivative of $h(x) = \tan(x)$ using trigonometric identities. Explain the steps involved in your calculation. \textbf{Part 2: Apply the Quotient Rule to Derivatives of Trigonometric Functions}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Use the quotient rule to find the derivative of the function $f(x) = \frac{\sin(x)}{\cos(x)}$. Simplify your result and identify the resulting trigonometric function.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Given the function $f(x) = \frac{\sec(x)}{\tan(x)}$, which of the following correctly represents its derivative?
    \item A. $\frac{\sec(x) \cdot \sec(x) \tan(x) - \sec(x) \cdot \sec(x) \sec^2(x)}{\tan^2(x)}$
    \item B. $\frac{\sec(x) \cdot \sec(x) - \sec(x) \cdot \tan(x)}{\tan^2(x)}$
    \item C. $\frac{\sec(x) \cdot (\sec(x) \tan(x)) - \sec(x) \cdot (\sec(x) \cdot \sec^2(x))}{\tan^2(x)}$
    \item D. $\frac{\sec(x) \cdot \sec(x) - \sec(x) \cdot \tan(x)}{\sec^2(x)}$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find the derivative of the function $f(x) = \frac{\csc(x)}{\sin(x)}$ using the quotient rule. Explain your steps and provide the simplified result. \textbf{Part 3: Differentiate Between Trigonometric Functions and Their Derivatives}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Explain the significance of the derivative of the sine function $\sin(x)$ and the cosine function $\cos(x)$ in the context of periodic motion. How are these derivatives applied in practical problems?
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following correctly describes the derivative of the tangent function $\tan(x)$?
    \item A. The derivative of $\tan(x)$ is $\sec^2(x)$, which represents the rate of change of the tangent function with respect to $x$.
    \item B. The derivative of $\tan(x)$ is $\sec(x)$, which represents the rate of change of the tangent function with respect to $x$.
    \item C. The derivative of $\tan(x)$ is $\sin(x)$, which represents the rate of change of the tangent function with respect to $x$.
    \item D. The derivative of $\tan(x)$ is $\cos(x)$, which represents the rate of change of the tangent function with respect to $x$.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Compare and contrast the derivatives of the functions $\sin(x)$ and $\cos(x)$. Discuss how the derivatives of these trigonometric functions are used in different applications, such as in physics or engineering.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{The Chain Rule}
\label{sec:the-chain-rule}
%%===============================================================

The \textbf{chain rule} is a method to compute the derivative of the
functional composition of two or more functions.

If a function $f$ depends on a variable $u$ , which in turn depends on
another variable $x$ , that is $f=y\bigl(u(x)\bigr)$ , then the rate of
change of $f$ with respect to $x$ can be computed as the rate of change
of $y$ with respect to $u$ multiplied by the rate of change of $u$ with
respect to $x$ .

 \textbf{Chain Rule}
 If a function $f$ is composed to two differentiable functions $y(x)$ 
 and $u(x)$ , so that $f(x)=y\bigl(u(x)\bigr)$ , then $f(x)$ is      
 differentiable and,                                                 
 $$\frac{df}{dx}=\frac{dy}{du}\cdot\frac{du}{dx}$$ 

The method is called the "chain rule" because it can be applied
sequentially to as many functions as are nested inside one another. For
example, if $f$ is a function of $g$ which is in turn a function of $h$
, which is in turn a function of $x$ , that is

$$f\bigl(g(h(x))\bigr)$$

the derivative of $f$ with respect to $x$ is given by

$\frac{df}{dx}=\frac{df}{dg}\cdot\frac{dg}{dh}\cdot\frac{dh}{dx}$ and so on. 

A useful mnemonic is to think of the differentials as individual
entities that can be canceled algebraically, such as

$$\frac{df}{dx}=\frac{df}{\cancel{dg}}\cdot\frac{\cancel{dg}}{\cancel{dh}}\cdot\frac{\cancel{dh}}{dx}$$

However, keep in mind that this trick comes about through a clever
choice of notation rather than through actual algebraic cancellation.

The chain rule has broad applications in physics, chemistry, and
engineering, as well as being used to study related rates in many
disciplines. The chain rule can also be generalized to multiple
variables in cases where the nested functions depend on more than one
variable.

\paragraph{Examples}


\paragraph{Example I}


Suppose that a mountain climber ascends at a rate of $0.5\frac{km}{h}$ .
The temperature is lower at higher elevations; suppose the rate by which
it decreases is $6^\circ C$ per kilometer. To calculate the decrease in
air temperature per hour that the climber experiences, one multiplies
$\frac{6^\circ C}{km}$ by $0.5\frac{km}{h}$ , to obtain
$\frac{3^\circ C}{h}$ . This calculation is a typical chain rule
application.

\paragraph{Example II}


Consider the function $f(x)=(x^2+1)^3$ . It follows from the chain rule
that

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=(x^2+1)^3 \hspace{1cm} \text{Function to differentiate} \\\u(x)&=x^2+1 \hspace{1cm} \text{Define $u(x)$ as inside function} \\\f(x)&=u(x)^3 \hspace{1cm} \text{Express $f(x)$ in terms of $u(x)$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{df}{du}\cdot\frac{du}{dx} \hspace{1cm} \text{Express chain rule applicable here} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{d}{du}u^3\cdot\frac{d}{dx}(x^2+1) \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute in $f(u)$ and $u(x)$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=3u^2\cdot2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Compute derivatives with power rule} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=3(x^2+1)^2\cdot 2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute $u(x)$ back in terms of $x$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=6x(x^2+1)^2 \hspace{1cm} \text{Simplify.}
\end{aligned}$$

\paragraph{Example III}


In order to differentiate the trigonometric function

$$f(x)=\sin(x^2)$$ 

one can write:

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=\sin(x^2) \hspace{1cm} \text{Function to differentiate} \\\u(x)&=x^2 \hspace{1cm} \text{Define $u(x)$ as inside function} \\\f(x)&=\sin(u) \hspace{1cm} \text{Express $f(x)$ in terms of $u(x)$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{df}{du}\cdot\frac{du}{dx} \hspace{1cm} \text{Express chain rule applicable here} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{d}{du}\sin(u)\cdot\frac{d}{dx}(x^2) \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute in $f(u)$ and $u(x)$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\cos(u)\cdot 2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Evaluate derivatives} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\cos(x^2)\cdot 2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute $u$ in terms of $x.$}
\end{aligned}$$

\paragraph{Example IV: absolute value}


The chain rule can be used to differentiate $|x|$ , the absolute value
function:

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=|x| \hspace{1cm} \text{Function to differentiate} \\\f(x)&=\sqrt{x^2} \hspace{1cm} \text{Equivalent function} \\\u(x)&=x^2 \hspace{1cm} \text{Define $u(x)$ as inside function} \\\f(x)&=u(x)^\frac12 \hspace{1cm} \text{Express $f(x)$ in terms of $u(x)$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{df}{du}\cdot\frac{du}{dx} \hspace{1cm} \text{Express chain rule applicable here} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{d}{du}u^\frac12\cdot\frac{d}{dx}(x^2) \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute in $f(u)$ and $u(x)$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{u^{-\frac12}}{2}\cdot 2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Compute derivatives with power rule} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{(x^2)^{-\frac12}}{2}\cdot 2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute $u(x)$ back in terms of $x$} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{x}{\sqrt{x^2}} \hspace{1cm} \text{Simplify} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{x}{|x|} \hspace{1cm} \text{Express $\sqrt{x^2}$ as absolute value.}
\end{aligned}$$

\paragraph{Example V: three nested functions}


The method is called the "chain rule" because it can be applied
sequentially to as many functions as are nested inside one another. For
example, if $f\bigl(g(h(x))\bigr)=e^{\sin(x^2)}$ , sequential
application of the chain rule yields the derivative as follows (we make
use of the fact that $\frac{d}{dx}e^x=e^x$ , which will be proved in a
later section):

$$\begin{aligned}
f(x)&=e^{\sin(x^2)}=e^g \hspace{1cm} \text{Original (outermost) function} \\\h(x)&=x^2 \hspace{1cm} \text{Define $h(x)$ as innermost function} \\\g(x)&=\sin(h)&=\sin(x^2) \hspace{1cm} \text{$g(h)=sin(h)$ as middle function} \\\\frac{df}{dx}&=\frac{df}{dg}\cdot\frac{dg}{dh}\cdot\frac{dh}{dx} \hspace{1cm} \text{Express chain rule applicable here} \\\\frac{df}{dg}&=e^g=e^{\sin(x^2)} \hspace{1cm} \text{Differentiate f(g)} \\\\frac{dg}{dh}&=\cos(h)=\cos(x^2) \hspace{1cm} \text{Differentiate $g(h)$} \\\\frac{dh}{dx}&=2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Differentiate $h(x)$} \\\\frac{d}{dx}e^{\sin(x^2)}&= e^{\sin(x^2)}\cdot\cos(x^2)\cdot 2x \hspace{1cm} \text{Substitute into chain rule.}
\end{aligned}$$

\paragraph{Proof of the chain rule}


Suppose $y$ is a function of $u$ which is a function of $x$ (it is
assumed that $y$ is differentiable at $u$ and $x$ , and $u$ is
differentiable at $x$ . To prove the chain rule we use the definition of
the derivative.

$$\frac{dy}{dx}=\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$$ 

We now multiply $\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}$ by $\frac{\Delta u}{\Delta u}$ and
perform some algebraic manipulation.

$$\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta x}=\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta u}\cdot\frac{\Delta u}{\Delta x}=\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta u}\cdot\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta u}{\Delta x}=\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta u}\cdot\frac{du}{dx}$$

Note that as $\Delta x$ approaches $0$ , $\Delta u$ also approaches $0.$ So taking the limit as of a function as $\Delta x$ approaches $0$ is
the same as taking its limit as $\Delta u$ approaches $0$ . Thus

$$\lim\_{\Delta x\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta u}=\lim\_{\Delta u\to0}\frac{\Delta y}{\Delta u}=\frac{dy}{du}$$

So we have

$$\frac{dy}{dx}=\frac{dy}{du}\cdot\frac{du}{dx}$$

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Apply the Chain Rule to Compute Derivatives:} Students will be able to use the chain rule to compute the derivatives of composite functions. They should be able to correctly differentiate functions of the form $f(x) = y(u(x))$ and apply the chain rule to functions with multiple nested layers.
    \item \textbf{Demonstrate the Chain Rule with Trigonometric and Algebraic Functions:} Students will be able to differentiate functions involving trigonometric and algebraic expressions by expressing them in terms of intermediate functions.
    \item \textbf{Interpret and Apply the Chain Rule in Real-World Contexts:} Students will be able to apply the chain rule to solve practical problems involving rates of change. For example, they should be able to calculate the rate at which a quantity changes in relation to another variable by using the chain rule, such as determining the rate of temperature change experienced by a mountain climber based on their ascent rate and the temperature gradient.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Apply the Chain Rule to Compute Derivatives}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Differentiate the function $f(x) = \sqrt{3x^2 + 2x}$ using the chain rule. Show your work and include the final result.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Given the function $f(x) = \sin(2x^3 + 5x)$, what is the derivative with respect to $x$?
    \item A. $\cos(2x^3 + 5x) \cdot (6x^2 + 5)$
    \item B. $\cos(2x^3 + 5x) \cdot (6x^2 - 5)$
    \item C. $\cos(2x^3 + 5x) \cdot (6x^2 + 10)$
    \item D. $\cos(2x^3 + 5x) \cdot (2x^3 + 5)$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the chain rule to differentiate the function $f(x) = \ln(4x^2 + 3)$. Describe the steps you used in applying the chain rule. \textbf{Part 2: Demonstrate the Chain Rule with Trigonometric and Algebraic Functions}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Differentiate the function $f(x) = \tan(x^2 + 1)$. Apply the chain rule and show all steps leading to your final answer.
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find the derivative of the function $f(x) = \sin^2(x)$ using the chain rule. Explain your process and simplify the result.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} For the function $f(x) = e^{\cos(x^2)}$, which of the following represents the derivative with respect to $x$?
    \item A. $-e^{\cos(x^2)} \cdot \sin(x^2) \cdot 2x$
    \item B. $e^{\cos(x^2)} \cdot \sin(x^2) \cdot 2x$
    \item C. $-e^{\cos(x^2)} \cdot \cos(x^2) \cdot 2x$
    \item D. $e^{\cos(x^2)} \cdot \cos(x^2) \cdot 2x$ \textbf{Part 3: Interpret and Apply the Chain Rule in Real-World Contexts}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Suppose the temperature at a height $h$ is given by $T(h) = 20 - 0.01h$, where $h$ is the height in meters. If a climber ascends at a rate of $1$ meter per minute, use the chain rule to determine the rate at which the temperature changes with respect to time.
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Consider a sphere whose radius $r$ changes over time according to the function $r(t) = 3t^2 + 2t$. If the volume of the sphere is given by $V = \frac{4}{3} \pi r^3$, find the rate at which the volume is changing when $t = 2$ minutes. Apply the chain rule to find your answer.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} If the distance between two cars is given by $d(t) = 100 - 5t$ and one car is moving away from the other at a rate of $5$ meters per second, what is the rate at which the distance between the two cars is changing at $t = 10$ seconds?
    \item A. $-5$ meters per second
    \item B. $5$ meters per second
    \item C. $-10$ meters per second
    \item D. $10$ meters per second
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Derivatives of Inverse Functions}
\label{sec:derivatives-of-inverse-functions}
%%===============================================================

In mathematics, the \textbf{inverse} of a function $y = f(x)$ is a function
that, in some fashion, "undoes" the effect of $f.$ The inverse of $f$
is denoted as $f^{-1},$ where $f^{-1}(y) = x$ if and only if $f(x) = y.$

Their two derivatives, assuming they exist, are reciprocal, as the
Leibniz notation suggests; that is:

$$\frac{dx}{dy}\,\cdot\, \frac{dy}{dx} = 1.$$

This relation is obtained by differentiating the equation $f^{-1}(y)=x$
in terms of $x$ and applying the chain rule, yielding that:

$$\frac{dx}{dy}\,\cdot\, \frac{dy}{dx} = \frac{dx}{dx}$$

considering that the derivative of $x$ with respect to \textit{$x$} is 1.

Writing explicitly the dependence of $y$ on $x,$ and the point at which
the differentiation takes place, the formula for the derivative of the
inverse becomes (in Lagrange's notation):

$$\left[f^{-1}\right]'(a)=\frac{1}{f'\left( f^{-1}(a) \right)}$.$

This formula holds in general whenever $f$ is continuous and injective
on an interval $I,$ with $f$ being differentiable at
$f^{-1}(a)$($\in I$) and where $f'(f^{-1}(a)) \ne 0.$ The same formula
is also equivalent to the expression

$$\mathcal{D}\left[f^{-1}\right]=\frac{1}{(\mathcal{D} f)\circ \left(f^{-1}\right)},$$

where $\mathcal{D}$ denotes the unary derivative operator (on the space
of functions) and $\circ$ denotes function composition.

Geometrically, a function and inverse function have graphs that are
reflections, in the line $y=x.$ This reflection operation turns the
gradient of any line into its reciprocal.

Assuming that $f$ has an inverse in a neighbourhood of $x$ and that its
derivative at that point is non-zero, its inverse is guaranteed to be
differentiable at $x$ and have a derivative given by the above formula.

![Rule:${\color{CornflowerBlue}{f'}}(x) = \frac{1}{{\color{Salmon}{(f^{-1})'}}({\color{Blue}{f}}(x))}$Example for arbitrary $x\_0 \approx 5.8$:${\color{CornflowerBlue}{f'}}(x\_0) = \frac{1}{4}$${\color{Salmon}{(f^{-1})'}}({\color{Blue}{f}}(x\_0)) = 4\textasciitilde{}$](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Umkehrregel\_2.png/250px-Umkehrregel\_2.png)

\paragraph{Examples}


$y = x^2$ (for positive \textit{x} has inverse $x = \sqrt{y}.$

$$\frac{dy}{dx} = 2x; \frac{dx}{dy} = \frac{1}{2\sqrt{y}}=\frac{1}{2x}$$

$$\frac{dy}{dx}\,\cdot\,\frac{dx}{dy} = 2x \cdot\frac{1}{2x}  =  1.$$

At $x=0,$ however, there is a problem: the graph of the square root
function becomes vertical, corresponding to a horizontal tangent for the
square function.

$y = e^x$ (for real \textit{x} has inverse $x = \ln{y}$ (for positive $y$)

$$\frac{dy}{dx} = e^x; \frac{dx}{dy} = \frac{1}{y}$$

$$\frac{dy}{dx}\,\cdot\,\frac{dx}{dy}  =  e^x \cdot \frac{1}{y}  =  \frac{e^x}{e^x}  =  1$$

\end{itemize}
    Functions}
