\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Application of Basic Integral Properties} Students will be able to apply basic integral properties, such as the Constant Rule and Sum/Difference Rule, to compute indefinite integrals of functions involving constants and sums or differences of functions.
    \item \textbf{Evaluation of Antiderivatives for Common Functions} Students will be able to determine and use the antiderivatives of common functions, including polynomials, exponential functions, and trigonometric functions, applying rules such as the Power Rule, and recognizing the integrals of $\frac{1}{x}$ and $e^x$.
    \item \textbf{Finding the Area Between Two Curves} Students will be able to find the area between two curves by setting up and evaluating definite integrals, and can distinguish between integrating with respect to the $x$-axis and $y$-axis based on the given functions and interval.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Application of Basic Integral Properties}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Apply the Constant Rule to find the integral of the constant function $f(x) = 7$. Express your result as an indefinite integral.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following integrals correctly applies the Sum Rule?
    \item A. $\int (3x^2 + 5) \, dx = \int 3x^2 \, dx + \int 5 \, dx$
    \item B. $\int (3x^2 + 5) \, dx = \int (3x^2) + \int 5 \, dx$
    \item C. $\int (3x^2 + 5) \, dx = 3 \int x^2 \, dx + \int 5 \, dx$
    \item D. $\int (3x^2 + 5) \, dx = \int 3x^2 \, dx + 5 \int \, dx$
    \item \textbf{Fill-in-the-Blanks:} To compute the integral of $f(x) = 2x + 4 - 3x^2$, first use the __________ rule to separate the integral into simpler parts. Then, apply the __________ rule to integrate each term individually. \textbf{Part 2: Evaluation of Antiderivatives for Common Functions}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find the antiderivative of the function $f(x) = x^4 - 3x + \frac{1}{x}$. Express your result including the constant of integration.
    \item \textbf{Problem Solving:} Compute the indefinite integral of $f(x) = e^x \sin(x)$. Use integration by parts if necessary and show all steps.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following integrals is correctly evaluated for $f(x) = \frac{1}{x}$?
    \item A. $\int \frac{1}{x} \, dx = \ln|x| + C$
    \item B. $\int \frac{1}{x} \, dx = \ln(x) + C$
    \item C. $\int \frac{1}{x} \, dx = e^x + C$
    \item D. $\int \frac{1}{x} \, dx = \frac{1}{2}x^2 + C$ \textbf{Part 3: Finding the Area Between Two Curves}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Given the functions $f(x) = x^2$ and $g(x) = x$, find the area between these two curves over the interval $[0, 1]$. Set up the definite integral needed for this calculation.
    \item \textbf{Problem Solving:} Calculate the area between the curves $y = e^x$ and $y = x^2$ over the interval $[0, 1]$. Include the steps to set up and evaluate the definite integral.
    \item \textbf{Conceptual Understanding (Short Answer):} When finding the area between two curves, how do you decide whether to integrate with respect to $x$ or $y$? Provide an example where integrating with respect to $y$ is necessary, and explain why.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Integration by Substitution}
\label{sec:integration-by-substitution}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{Integration by Substitution}


There is a theorem that will help you with substitution for integration.
It is called \textbf{Change of Variables for Definite Integrals}.

what the theorem looks like is this

$$\int\_{a}^{b} f(x)\operatorname {d}x = \int\_{\alpha}^{\beta} f(g(u))g\prime (u)\operatorname {d}u$$

In order to get $\alpha$ you must plug \textbf{a}
into the function \textbf{g} and to get $\beta$ you must plug
\textbf{b} into the function \textbf{g}.

The tricky part is trying to identify what you want to make your
\textbf{u} to be. Some times substitution will not
be enough and you will have to use the rules for integration by parts.
That will be covered in a different section

\paragraph{Steps}


$\begin(aligned}
\int\limits\_{x=a}^{x=b}f(x)dx\&=\int\limits\_{x=a}^{x=b} f(x)\ \frac{du}{du}\ dx \hspace{1cm} \text{(1) i.e.} \hspace{1cm} \frac{du}{du}=1$ \\\\&=\int\limits\_{x=a}^{x=b}{\left(f(x)\ \frac{dx}{du}\right)\left(\frac{du}{dx}\right)}\ dx \hspace{1cm} \text{(2) i.e.} \hspace{1cm} \frac{dx}{du}\cdot\frac{du}{dx}=1 \\\\&=\int\limits\_{x=a}^{x=b}\left(f(x)\ \frac{dx}{du}\right)g'(x)\ dx \hspace{1cm} \text{(3) i.e.} \hspace{1cm} \frac{du}{dx}=g'(x) \\\\&=\int\limits\_{x=a}^{x=b}h(g(x))g'(x)dx \hspace{1cm} \text{(4) i.e. Now equate} \hspace{1cm} \left(f(x)\ \frac{dx}{du}\right) \hspace{1cm} \text{with} \hspace{1cm} h(g(x)) \\\\&=\int\limits\_{x=a}^{x=b}h(u)g'(x)dx \hspace{1cm} \text{(5) i.e.} \hspace{1cm} g(x)=u \\\\&=\int\limits\_{u=g(a)}^{u=g(b)}h(u)du \hspace{1cm} \text{(6) i.e.} du=\frac{du}{dx}dx=g'(x)dx \\\\&=\int\limits\_{u=c}^{u=d}h(u)du \hspace{1cm} \text{(7) i.e. We have achieved our desired result}
\end{aligned}$

\paragraph{Example 1}


$\int\_{0}^{2} x(x^2+1)^2 \operatorname {d}x$

Instead of making this a big polynomial we will just use the
substitution method.

\textbf{Step 1}

Identify your \textit{u}

Let $u = x^2+1$

\textbf{Step 2}

Identify $\operatorname {d}u$

$\operatorname {d}u = 2x\operatorname {d}x$

\textbf{Step 3}

Now we plug in our limits of integration to our \textit{u} to find our new limits of integration

When $x = 0, u =0^2 + 1 = 1$

and when $x = 2, u = 2^2 + 1 = 5$

Now our integration problem looks something like this

$$\frac {1}{2} \int\_{0}^{5} (x^2 + 1)^2 (2x)\operatorname {d}x$$


\textbf{Step 4}

write your new integration problem

When we plug in our \textit{u} it looks like

$$\frac {1}{2} \int\_{0}^{5} (u)^2 \operatorname {d}u$$

\textbf{Step 5}

Evaluate the Integral

$$\frac {1}{2} \left[\frac {1}{3} u^3 \right]\_{0}^{5}$$

$$\frac {1}{2} \left[\left(\frac {1}{3} * 5^3 \right) - \left(\frac {1}{3} * 0^3 \right)\right]$$

$$\frac {1}{2} \left[\frac {1}{3} * 125 \right]$$

$$\frac {1}{2} \left[\frac {125}{3}\right]$$

$$\frac {125}{6}$$

As you can see this all simplified fairly nice. Using substitution will be hard, for most people, at first. Once you get the hang of
doing this it should come to you faster and faster each time.

\paragraph{Example 2}


$$\int 3x^2(x^3+1)^5dx$$

we see that $3x^2$ is the derivative of $x^3+1$ . Letting

$$u=x^3+1$$

we have

$$\frac{du}{dx}=3x^2$$

or, in order to apply it to the integral,

$$du=3x^2dx$$

With this we may write

$$\int 3x^2(x^3+1)^5dx=\int u^5du=\frac{u^6}{6}+C=\frac{(x^3+1)^6}{6}+C$$

Note that it was not necessary that we had \textit{exactly}the derivative of $u$ in our integrand.
It would have been sufficient to have any constant multiple of the derivative.

For instance, to treat the integral

$$\int x^4\sin(x^5)dx$$

we may let $u=x^5$ . Then

$$du=5x^4dx$$ and so

$$\frac{du}{5}=x^4dx$$

the right-hand side of which is a factor of our integrand. Thus,

$$\int x^4\sin(x^5)dx=\int\frac{\sin(u)}{5}du=-\frac{\cos(u)}{5}+C=-\frac{\cos(x^5)}{5}+C$$

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Apply the Change of Variables Theorem}: Students will be able to use the Change of Variables theorem to transform a definite integral into a new variable $u$ and accurately adjust the limits of integration accordingly.
    \item \textbf{Perform Substitution for Integration}: Students will be able to identify appropriate substitutions for integrals, compute the derivative $\frac{du}{dx}$, and convert the integral into terms of $u$ to simplify and solve it.
    \item \textbf{Evaluate Definite Integrals Using Substitution}: Students will be able to evaluate definite integrals by substituting variables, simplifying the integrand, and adjusting the limits of integration, then compute the integral using standard integration techniques.
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Apply the Change of Variables Theorem}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Apply the Change of Variables theorem to transform the integral $$\int_{0}^{1} (3x^2 + 2) \, dx$$ using the substitution $u = x^3$. Find the new limits of integration and the transformed integral in terms of $u$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} For the integral $$\int_{-2}^{2} (4 - x^2) \, dx$$, if you use the substitution $u = x^2$, which of the following is the correct change of limits for $u$?
    \item A. $u$ changes from $4$ to $0$
    \item B. $u$ changes from $0$ to $4$
    \item C. $u$ changes from $-4$ to $4$
    \item D. $u$ changes from $-2$ to $2$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the Change of Variables theorem to find the transformed integral of $\int_{1}^{e} \frac{1}{x \ln(x)} \, dx$ with the substitution $u = \ln(x)$. Provide the new limits of integration and the transformed integral. \textbf{Part 2: Perform Substitution for Integration}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Evaluate the integral $$\int \frac{2x}{x^2 + 1} \, dx$$ by using the substitution $u = x^2 + 1$. Compute $\frac{du}{dx}$, substitute into the integral, and simplify.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} If you have the integral $$\int \sin(3x) \, dx$$, which substitution would simplify the integral most effectively?
    \item A. $u = \sin(3x)$
    \item B. $u = 3x$
    \item C. $u = \cos(3x)$
    \item D. $u = 3 \sin(x)$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Use the substitution $u = 2x - 1$ to evaluate the integral $\int (2x - 1) e^{2x - 1} \, dx$. Compute $\frac{du}{dx}$, transform the integral, and solve. \textbf{Part 3: Evaluate Definite Integrals Using Substitution}
    \item \textbf{Calculation:} Evaluate the definite integral $\int_{0}^{\pi/2} \cos^2(x) \sin(x) \, dx$ using the substitution $u = \sin(x)$. Adjust the limits of integration accordingly and compute the integral.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} For the integral $$\int_{1}^{4} \frac{1}{\sqrt{x - 1}} \, dx$$, which substitution is appropriate to simplify the integral?
    \item A. $u = \sqrt{x - 1}$
    \item B. $u = x - 1$
    \item C. $u = \frac{1}{\sqrt{x - 1}}$
    \item D. $u = \frac{1}{x - 1}$
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Evaluate the definite integral $\int_{0}^{1} x e^{x^2} \, dx$ using the substitution $u = x^2$. Provide the adjusted limits of integration and the final result.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Integrals Involving Exponential and Logarithmic Functions}
\label{sec:integrals-involving-exponential-and-logarithmic-functions}
%%===============================================================

\paragraph{Logarithm Function}


We shall first look at the irrational number $e$ in order to show its
special properties when used with derivatives of exponential and
logarithm functions. As mentioned before in the Algebra section, the
value of $e$ is approximately $e\approx2.718282$ but it may also be
calculated as the Infinite Limit:

$$e=\lim_{n\to\infty}\left(1+\frac{1}{n}\right)^n$$

Now we find the derivative of $\ln(x)$ using the formal definition of
the derivative:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\ln(x)=\lim_{h\to0}\frac{\ln(x+h)-\ln(x)}{h}=\lim_{h\to0}\frac{1}{h}\ln\left(\frac{x+h}{x}\right)=\lim_{h\to0}\ln\left(\frac{x+h}{x}\right)^\frac{1}{h}$$

Let $n=\frac{x}{h}$ . Note that as $n\to\infty$ , we get $h\to0$ . So we
can redefine our limit as:

$$\lim_{n\to\infty}\ln\left(1+\frac{1}{n}\right)^\frac{n}{x}=\frac{1}{x}\ln\left(\lim_{n\to\infty}\left(1+\frac{1}{n}\right)^n\right)=\frac{1}{x}\ln(e)=\frac{1}{x}$$

Here we could take the natural logarithm outside the limit because it
doesn\'t have anything to do with the limit (we could have chosen not
to). We then substituted the value of $e$ .

\textbf{Derivative of the Natural Logarithm}
$$\frac{d}{dx}\ln(x)=\frac{1}{x}$$

If we wanted, we could go through that same process again for a
generalized base, but it is easier just to use properties of logs and
realize that:

$$\log_a(x)=\frac{\ln(x)}{\ln(a)}$$

Since $\frac{1}{\ln(a)}$ is a constant, we can just take it outside of
the derivative:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\log_a(x)=\frac{1}{\ln(a)}\cdot\frac{d}{dx}\ln(x)$$

Which leaves us with the generalized form of:

\textbf{Derivative of the Logarithm}
$$\frac{d}{dx}\log_a(x)=\frac{1}{\ln(a)x}$$

An alternative approach to derivative of the logarithm refers to the
original expression of the logarithm as quadrature of the hyperbola \textit{y} = 1/\textit{x}.

\paragraph{Exponential Function}


We shall take two different approaches to finding the derivative of
$\ln(e^x)$ . The first approach:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\ln(e^x)=\frac{d}{dx}x=1$$

The second approach:

$$\frac{d}{dx}\ln(e^x)=\frac{1}{e^x}\left(\frac{d}{dx}e^x\right)$$

Note that in the second approach we made some use of the chain rule. Thus:

$$\frac{1}{e^x}\left(\frac{d}{dx}e^x\right)=1$$

$$\frac{d}{dx}e^x=e^x$$

so that we have proved the following rule:

\textbf{Derivative of the exponential function}
$$\frac{d}{dx}e^x=e^x$$

Now that we have derived a specific case, let us extend things to the
general case. Assuming that $a$ is a positive real constant, we wish to
calculate:

$$\frac{d}{dx}a^x$$

One of the oldest tricks in mathematics is to break a problem down into
a form that we already know we can handle. Since we have already
determined the derivative of $e^x$ , we will attempt to rewrite $a^x$ in
that form.

Using that $e^{\ln(c)}=c$ and that $\ln(a^b)=b\cdot\ln(a)$ , we find
that:

$$a^x=e^{\ln(a)x}$$

Thus, we simply apply the chain rule:

$$\frac{d}{dx}e^{\ln(a)x}=e^{\ln(a)x}\cdot\frac{d}{dx}[\ln(a)x]=\ln(a)a^x$$

\textbf{Derivative of the exponential function}
$$\frac{d}{dx}a^x=\ln(a)a^x$$          

\paragraph{Exponential \& Logarithm Integrals}


Regarding the derivatives of exponentials and logarithms, we know that:

\begin{itemize}
    \item $\dfrac{d}{dx} e^x = e^x$
    \item $\dfrac{d}{dx} \ln x = \frac{1}{x}$
\end{itemize}

Reversing these, we get:

\begin{itemize}
    \item $\int e^x = e^x + c$
    \item $\int \frac{1}{x} = \ln |x|$
\end{itemize}

The natural logarithm takes a modulus input so that it can handle
negative numbers.

Note that similar rules apply to any linear expressions that may be
composed with these functions:

\begin{itemize}
    \item $\int e^{ax+b} = \frac{1}{a}e^{ax+b} + c$
    \item $\int \frac{1}{ax+b} = \frac{1}{a}\ln|ax+b|$
\end{itemize}

\paragraph{Resources}


\begin{itemize}

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Understand the Derivative of the Natural Logarithm:} Students will be able to derive and explain the formula for the derivative of the natural logarithm function, specifically showing that $\frac{d}{dx} \ln(x) = \frac{1}{x}$ using the formal definition of the derivative.
    \item \textbf{Apply Logarithm Properties to Derivatives:} Students will be able to apply the properties of logarithms to find the derivative of logarithmic functions with any positive base $a$, demonstrating that $\frac{d}{dx} \log_a(x) = \frac{1}{\ln(a)x}.$
    \item \textbf{Differentiate Exponential Functions and Integrate Exponential and Logarithmic Functions:} Students will be able to differentiate exponential functions of the form $e^x$ and $a^x$, showing that $\frac{d}{dx} e^x = e^x$ and $\frac{d}{dx} a^x = \ln(a) a^x,$ and apply the corresponding integration rules for exponential and logarithmic functions, including finding antiderivatives like $\int e^x \, dx = e^x + C$ and $\int \frac{1}{x} \, dx = \ln|x| + C.$
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Understand the Derivative of the Natural Logarithm}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Using the formal definition of the derivative, derive the formula for the derivative of the natural logarithm function $f(x) = \ln(x)$. Show all steps in your derivation.
    \item \textbf{True/False:} The derivative of $\ln(x)$ is $\frac{1}{x}$ for all $x > 0$.
    \item A. True
    \item B. False
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Explain why the derivative of $\ln(x)$ is not defined for $x \leq 0$. Discuss the implications for the domain of the function and its derivative. \textbf{Part 2: Apply Logarithm Properties to Derivatives}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Find the derivative of the function $f(x) = \log_3(x^2 + 1)$. Apply the property of logarithms to simplify the differentiation process and show your work.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Given $f(x) = \log_a(x)$, which of the following correctly represents the derivative of $f(x)$?
    \item A. $\frac{d}{dx} \log_a(x) = \frac{1}{x \ln(a)}$
    \item B. $\frac{d}{dx} \log_a(x) = \frac{\ln(a)}{x}$
    \item C. $\frac{d}{dx} \log_a(x) = \frac{1}{x \ln(a)}$
    \item D. $\frac{d}{dx} \log_a(x) = \frac{a}{x}$
    \item \textbf{Problem Solving:} Differentiate the function $f(x) = \log_5(2x + 3)$. Use logarithm properties and show the step-by-step process of differentiation. \textbf{Part 3: Differentiate Exponential Functions and Integrate Exponential and Logarithmic Functions}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Differentiate the function $f(x) = 3e^x - 4a^x$, where $a$ is a positive constant. Show the steps and include the results.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following integrals is correctly evaluated?
    \item A. $\int e^x \, dx = e^x + C$
    \item B. $\int e^x \, dx = e^x + \ln(x) + C$
    \item C. $\int e^x \, dx = e^{x+1} + C$
    \item D. $\int e^x \, dx = e^{x-1} + C$
    \item \textbf{Problem Solving:} Compute the integral $\int \frac{1}{x} \, dx$. Include the process to arrive at the antiderivative and express the result in terms of $x$ and the constant of integration.
\end{enumerate}

%%===============================================================
\section{Integrals Resulting in Inverse Trigonometric Functions}
\label{sec:integrals-resulting-in-inverse-trigonometric-functions}
%%===============================================================

$\int\frac{du}{\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}} = \arcsin \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$

$\int\frac{du}{a^2+u^2} =\dfrac{1}{a}\arctan \left(\dfrac{u}{a}\right) + C$

$\int\frac{du}{u\sqrt{u^2 - a^2}} =\frac{1}{a}\operatorname{arcsec} \left(\dfrac{|u|}{a}\right) + C$

\paragraph{Example 1}


Evaluate the integral

$\int\dfrac{dx}{\sqrt{4 - 9x^2}}.$

\textbf{Solution}

Substitute $u=3x.$ Then $du=3dx$ and we have

$$\int\dfrac{dx}{\sqrt{4 - 9x^2}}=\dfrac{1}{3}\int\dfrac{du}{\sqrt{4 - u^2}}.$$

Applying the formula with $a=2,$ we obtain

$$\int\dfrac{dx}{\sqrt{4 - 9x^2}}=\dfrac{1}{3}\int\dfrac{du}{\sqrt{4 - u^2}}=\dfrac{1}{3}\arcsin \left(\dfrac{u}{2}\right)+C=\dfrac{1}{3}\arcsin \left(\dfrac{3x}{2}\right)+C.$$

\paragraph{Example 2}


Evaluate $\int \frac{4-x}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}.$

\textbf{Solution}

This integral requires two different methods to evaluate it. We get to those methods by splitting up the integral:

$$\int \frac{4-x}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}  = \int \frac{4}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}  - \int \frac{x}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}$$

The first integral is handled straightforward; the second integral is
handled by substitution, with $u = 16-x^2.$ We handle each separately.

$\int \frac{4}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}  = 4\arcsin\frac{x}{4} + C.$

$\int\frac{x}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}$: Set $u = 16-x^2,$ so $\text{du}  = -2x\text{dx}$ and $x\text{dx}  = -\text{du} /2.$ We have

$\begin{aligned}
\int\frac{x}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}\&=\int\frac{-\text{du} /2}{\sqrt{u}} \\\\ 
\&= -\frac12\int \frac{1}{\sqrt{u}}\text{du}  \\\\&= - \sqrt{u} + C\\ = -\sqrt{16-x^2} + C.
\end{aligned}$

Combining these together, we have

$$\int \frac{4-x}{\sqrt{16-x^2}}\text{dx}  = 4\arcsin\frac x4 + \sqrt{16-x^2}+C.$$

    Tutor

\subsection{Learning Objectives}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Evaluate Integrals Involving $\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}$:}
    \item Students will be able to evaluate integrals of the form $\int \frac{du}{\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}}$ and recognize that the result is $\arcsin \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C.$
    \item \textbf{Evaluate Integrals Involving $a^2 + u^2$:}
    \item Students will be able to evaluate integrals of the form $\int \frac{du}{a^2 + u^2}$ and apply the result to find that it is $\frac{1}{a} \arctan \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C.$
    \item \textbf{Evaluate Integrals Involving $u \sqrt{u^2 - a^2}$:}
    \item Students will be able to evaluate integrals of the form $\int \frac{du}{u \sqrt{u^2 - a^2}}$ and apply the result to determine that it is $\frac{1}{a} \arcsec \left(\frac{|u|}{a}\right) + C.$
\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Assessment Instrument}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \textbf{Part 1: Evaluate Integrals Involving $\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}$}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Evaluate the integral $\int \frac{du}{\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}}$ and provide the result, including the constant of integration $C$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following is the correct result for the integral $\int \frac{du}{\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}}$?
    \item A. $\frac{u}{a} + C$
    \item B. $\arctan \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item C. $\arcsin \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item D. $\arccos \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item \textbf{Conceptual Question:} Explain why the integral $\int \frac{du}{\sqrt{a^2 - u^2}}$ results in the function $\arcsin \left(\frac{u}{a}\right)$. What geometric or trigonometric interpretation can be applied to this result? \textbf{Part 2: Evaluate Integrals Involving $a^2 + u^2$}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Compute the integral $\int \frac{du}{a^2 + u^2}$ and express the result, including the constant of integration $C$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} Which of the following expressions represents the result of the integral $\int \frac{du}{a^2 + u^2}$?
    \item A. $\frac{u}{a^2} + C$
    \item B. $\frac{1}{a} \arctan \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item C. $\frac{1}{a} \arcsin \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item D. $\frac{1}{a} \arccos \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item \textbf{Problem Solving:} Verify the result of the integral $\int \frac{du}{a^2 + u^2}$ by differentiating the antiderivative you obtained. Ensure that it matches the integrand. \textbf{Part 3: Evaluate Integrals Involving $u \sqrt{u^2 - a^2}$}
    \item \textbf{Short Answer:} Evaluate the integral $\int \frac{du}{u \sqrt{u^2 - a^2}}$ and provide the result, including the constant of integration $C$.
    \item \textbf{Multiple Choice:} What is the correct result of the integral $\int \frac{du}{u \sqrt{u^2 - a^2}}$?
    \item A. $\frac{1}{a} \arctan \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item B. $\frac{1}{a} \arcsec \left(\frac{|u|}{a}\right) + C$
    \item C. $\frac{1}{a} \arcsin \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item D. $\frac{1}{a} \arccos \left(\frac{u}{a}\right) + C$
    \item \textbf{Conceptual Question:} Describe the process and rationale for obtaining the result $\frac{1}{a} \operatorname{arcsec} \left(\frac{|u|}{a}\right) + C$ for the integral $\int \frac{du}{u \sqrt{u^2 - a^2}}$. What substitution or trigonometric identities might simplify this integral?
\end{enumerate}
